3

Abstract. In this research monograph we exploit two binary
codes associated to a projective nodal surface (the strict code K
and, for even degree d, the extended code K′ ) to investigate the
‘Nodal Severi varieties’ F(d, ν) of nodal surfaces in P3 of degree d
and with ν nodes, and their incidence hierarchy, relating partial
smoothings to code shortenings.
Our first main result (the case of surfaces of degree d ≤ 3 being
elementary) solves a question which dates back over 100 years: it
says that the irreducible components of F(4, ν) are in bijection
with the isomorphism classes of their extended codes K′ , that these
are exactly all the 34 possible shortenings of the extended Kummer
′
code KKum
(ν ≤ 16), and that a component is in the closure of
another if and only if the code of the latter is a shortening of the
code of the former.
We then extend this result to the classification of all the other
varieties of nodal K3 surfaces, of all possible degrees d = 2h: again
the connected components of the Nodal Severi variety FK3 (d, ν)
of nodal K3’s of degree d and with ν nodes are in bijection with
the isomorphism classes of their extended codes K′ ; we classify the
codes K′ , showing that their occurrence depends on the congruence
class of d modulo 16; we also see that some unexpected families
occur.
In 1979 Beauville used coding theory to prove that µ(5), the
maximum number of nodes that a nodal surface in P3 of degree
5 can have, equals 31; hence the ‘Togliatti quintics’, the discriminants of Togliatti cubics (cubic hypersurfaces in P5 with 15 nodes)
are the quintics with µ(5) = 31 nodes.
For surfaces of degree d = 5 in P3 we determine (with one
possible exception which remains an open question) all the possible codes K of nodal quintics, and show that we do not get all
the codes with weights 16 and 20 satisfying the inequality pointed
out by Beauville. Moreover, for several cases of K, we show the
irreducibility of the corresponding open set of F(5, ν): in particular, using the previous results on nodal K3 surfaces, we show the
irreducibility of the respective families of Togliatti quintic surfaces
and of Togliatti cubic fourfolds in P5 .
The fourth main result, in the final part of this paper, shows
that a ‘Togliatti-like’ description holds for surfaces of degree 6 with
the maximum number of nodes µ(6) = 65: they are discriminants
of cubic hypersurfaces in P6 with 31 nodes (also of cubics with 32
nodes), and we have an irreducible 18-dimensional family of them.
For degree d = 6, our main result is based on these novel
auxiliary results:
1) the study of the half-even sets of nodes on sextic surfaces,
2) the investigation of discriminants of cubic hypersurfaces X,
and the description of the relation between the nodal singularities
of X and the nodal singularities of the discriminant surface Y ,
3) the computer assisted proof that, for ν = 65, both codes
K, K′ are uniquely determined,
4) the explicit description of these interesting codes, starting
from the Barth sextic with 65 nodes, ending up with its relation to
the Doro-Hall graph (in particular, we find explicit discriminantal
and determinantal realizations of the Barth sextic).
The codes of sextic surfaces are investigated, with a partial
classification in the case of ‘many’ nodes.

Introduction
In this paper we shall first of all consider irreducible hypersurfaces X of degree
d in complex projective space PN := PN
C : these are the zero sets of an irreducible
homogeneous polynomial F (x0 , . . . , xN ) of degree d,
X := {x := (x0 , . . . , xN ) ∈ PN |F (x0 , . . . , xN ) = 0}.
∂F
The singular locus is the subset Sing(X) := {x| ∂x
= 0 ∀i = 0, . . . , N }, and

development

P ∂F

A hypersurface X is said to be nodal if its singular points are just nodes.

Definition 1. We propose in this paper the name of Nodal Severi variety
Fn (d, ν) for the locally closed set, inside the projective space parametrizing all
hypersurfaces in Pn+1 , consisting of the nodal hypersurfaces X of degree d and
dimension n in Pn+1 having exactly ν nodes as singularities.
Severi in fact [Sev21] studied the case n = 1, where one has to add the
hypothesis that X is irreducible, and it turns out that F1 (d, ν) (which is nonempty
if and only if ν ≤ (d−1)(d−2)
), is irreducible (the assertion, made in an appendix,
2
Anhang F, of [Sev21], had an incomplete proof, see [Har86]).
Several authors (see for instance [CC99], [CD12]) extended these investigations to the case of nodal curves lying on smooth surfaces; here, instead, we mainly
want to focus on the case of hypersurfaces and varieties of dimension at least two.
We have then the following (mainly unanswered) questions:
Question 2. (1) basic question: when is Fn (d, ν) non empty ?
The above question relies on the fact that, for fixed dimension n, the number ν
will be bounded by a function of the degree d, and this leads to another question:
(2) defining
µn (d) := max{ν|Fn (d, ν) ̸= ∅},
determine µn (d) explicitly.
In turn, knowing the maximum possible number of nodes µn (d) that a hypersurface of degree d can have, does not answer the first question, since one may ask
a weaker question:
(3) in particular, for which values of (n, d) do we have the no-gaps phenomenon that Fn (d, ν) is non empty for all ν ≤ µn (d)?
In turn, once we know that such a Severi variety Fn (d, ν) is non empty, one
may ask
(4) when is Fn (d, ν) irreducible? How to determine its irreducible components?
(5) When is Fn (d, ν) smooth?

6

CONTENTS

Concerning the last question (5), one can give a sufficient condition for smoothness, namely the condition of unobstructedness (which will be used in the paper),
meaning that the Severi variety is the fibre of a submersion. This criterion is used
for instance by Dimca and Kloostermann [Dim17] [Klo17]1 to show that F2 (d, ν)
is smooth for d = 5, 6, 7, and has pure codimension ν in the space of degree d surfaces. In [Klo17] examples are given, showing that F2 (d, 4(d − 1)) has a nonempty
singular locus, for d ≥ 9.
Already quite interesting, difficult and wide open is the case of surfaces: n = 2.
In this case we shall often denote F2 (d, ν) simply by F(d, ν), and µ2 (d) by µ(d).
To give a vague idea of the state of the art, let us give the following definition:
Definition 3. Let X be a hypersurface of degree d in Pn+1 with isolated
singularities, and let

be the number of singular points, and

We can then consider the maximal number of singular points
sn (d) := max{s(X)|X ⊂ Pn+1 , deg(X) = d, | Sing(X)| < ∞},
and similarly Milnorn (d).
For n = 2 we use the simpler notation s(d) := sn (d), Milnor(d) := Milnor2 (d).
We clearly have a chain of inequalities:
µn (d) ≤ sn (d) ≤ Milnorn (d).
The inequality µn (d) ≤ Milnorn (d) is strict, since Miyaoka proved [Miya84]
µ(d) ≤ 94 d(d − 1)2 , while we can show by easy examples (Example 116) that
Milnor(d) ≥ 12 d(d − 1)2 .
Also the inequality sn (d) ≤ Milnorn (d) is strict (but not by such an asymptotically large difference as before) as we can see comparing general inequalities by
Bruce [Bru81] for sn (d) with these examples (see again Example 116).
Concerning the first inequality, one may ask, assuming that life is simple, the
following

We conclude this general discussion simply saying here that s(d) = µ(d) for
d ≤ 4, and that the exact values of µ(d) = µ2 (d) are known only for d ≤ 6,
µ(d) = 0, 1, 4, 16, 31, 65 for d = 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6.
• d = 1, 2: trivial;
• d = 3: so-called Cayley cubic, first apparently found by Schläfli, see
[Sch63], [Cre68], [Cay69];
• d = 4: Kummer surfaces [Kum64] (first examples found by Fresnel,
1822);
• d = 5: Togliatti quintics, see [Tog40] for the inequality µ(5) ≥ 31, and
Beauville [Bea80] for the inequality µ(5) ≤ 31;
• d = 6: Barth [Bar96] µ(6) ≥ 65, Jaffe and Ruberman [JR97] µ(6) ≤ 65
(the proof was then simplified by Jonny Wahl, see [Wah98] and [PT09]).
Also, it turns out that in this range d ≤ 6 (as we shall explain in this article)
the answer to questions 1 and 3 is that there are no gaps (in view of [CC82] and
[Roh86], [Roh87] the only new result here is for the case d = 5, see corollary 40):
Corollary 5. If n = 2, d ≤ 6, then we have the ‘no gaps’ phenomenon for
nodal surfaces: F2 (d, ν) is non-empty for all ν ≤ µ(d). This means: for each ν
with 1 ≤ ν ≤ µ(d) there exists a nodal surface X of degree d in P3 with precisely
ν nodes.
The answer to question 4 is classical for n = 2, d ≤ 3: the Nodal Severi varieties
are smooth and irreducible.
While the case d = 4, in spite of a lot of classical work ([Roh86], [Roh87],
[Jes16]), was until now open (see the remarks in the first three lines of page 349
of [End99]). We are here able to classify the irreducible components of F2 (4, ν),
for ν ≤ 16, we have indeed our main theorem for nodal quartics:

8

CONTENTS

Theorem 6. (I) The subset of the Nodal Severi variety F(4, ν) corresponding
to the nodal quartic surfaces with ν nodes and fixed binary code K′′ is a smooth
connected component.
(II) The possible codes K′′ appearing are all the shortenings of the extended
′′
Kummer code KKum
.
(III) A component of F(4, ν) is in the closure of another component of F(4, ν ′ )
if and only if ν ≥ ν ′ and the second code is a shortening of the first.
The main thrust of the above theorem is of being a conceptual statement, as
opposed to hardly readable and very long computer generated lists, but indeed it
leads to a very explicit classification, and the possible codes K′′ appearing are listed
in Section 1.8 (11 nontrivial codes in their minimal embedding); the classification2
shows for instance that the Nodal Severi variety F(4, ν) has exactly one irreducible
component for ν = 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 14, 15, 16, exactly two irreducible components
for ν = 6, 7, 13, exactly three irreducible components for ν = 8, 9, exactly four
irreducible components for ν = 11, 12, exactly five irreducible components for
ν = 10. Hence its stratification is made of 34 strata.
A direct consequence is:
Corollary 7. Let Y be a nodal quartic surface in P3 , with ν nodes, where
ν ≤ 15.
Then the fundamental group π1 (Y ∗ ) of its smooth locus is finite and isomorphic
to its code K ∼
= (Z/2)k .
For ν = 16 π1 (Y ∗ ) is the semidirect product Z4 ⋊ Z/2 corresponding to multiplication by −1.
The main difference from the case of curves is given here by topology: given a
nodal hypersurface X, we consider the nonsingular part of X, X ∗ := X \ Sing(X).
In the case of curves we just delete 2ν points from a Riemann surface of genus
g = (d−1)(d−2)
− ν, so the topology is detected by the integers d, ν.
2
For the case of surfaces Y , instead, we have four important topological invariants of the connected components of F(d, ν). The first two are the fundamental
group π1 (Y ∗ ), and its abelianization H1 (Y ∗ , Z), which is a vector space over Z/2
and a quotient of (Z/2)ν . The second two are similarly defined: letting Y ′ be
the complement in Y ∗ of a smooth hyperplane section, we have the fundamental
group π1 (Y ′ ), and its abelianization H1 (Y ′ , Z), a quotient of (Z/2)ν ⊕ (Z/d) (see
Corollary 18 and Corollary 26).
While the fundamental groups are intriguing groups, and difficult to compute for d ≥ 5, these homology groups are somewhat easier to calculate, and
through them coding theory comes into play, as first pioneered in the seminal
paper [Bea80].
2again, everything is done by hand using linear algebra.

INTRODUCTION

9

Definition 8. Let Y be a nodal surface in P3 , and let S be its singular set
S := Sing(Y ) = {P1 , . . . , Pν } :
then the strict code K ⊂ (Z/2)S associated to Y is defined as the image of the
injective linear map dual to the surjective linear map (Z/2)S → H1 (Y ∗ , Z).
If d is even, the extended code K′ ⊂ (Z/2)S ⊕ (Z/2)H associated to Y is
defined via the injective linear map dual the surjective linear map obtained from
the surjection (Z/2)S ⊕(Z/d)H → H1 (Y ′ , Z) by setting d2 H = 0. It is a bicoloured
code, as we are now going to explain.
In order to fully understand the statement of Theorem 6 above, one needs to
recall some basic concepts and results from coding theory, for this we refer to the
first section of Part 1.
The main use of coding theory is based on the determination of the cardinalities
of strictly even sets of nodes (weights of vectors in K), and of half-even sets of nodes
(vectors in the projection K′′ of K′ \ K inside FS2 ), which was initiated in [Cat81],
[Bea80], and continued in several papers ([CC97], [End98], [CT05]), and which
has lead to the complete classification of the K-weights for surfaces of degree up
to 6 (we did not yet achieve the complete classification for the K′ -weights of sextic
surfaces, but we obtain here, concerning the half-even sets, new results sufficient
for our purposes).
In degree d = 4, the weights for K can only be 8, 16, and those for K′′ can only
be 8, 16, 6, 10. While the classification of the codes K, even for more general K3
surfaces, is well known since long time, the classification of the codes K′ is a much
harder and newer task, because of the occurrence of more than 4 possible weights
(which depend on the congruence class of d modulo 8).
The main theorem for quartics, theorem 6, is based on several ingredients:
first of all elementary coding theory, then the relation between unobstructedness
and shortenings (theorem 36), then Nikulin’s theorems on primitive embeddings
of lattices in the K3 lattice [Nik79] and finally the Torelli theorem for K3 surfaces
(see [P-SS71] [Bea85]).
The relation with lattices occurs as follows: for nodal surfaces Y , with minimal
resolution Ỹ , the lattice Λ := H 2 (Ỹ , Z) contains the sublattice
M
L=
ZEi ⊕ ZH.
i
′

Now, the extended code K determines the saturation Lsat := Λ ∩ QL of L, which
is primitively embedded inside Λ := H 2 (Ỹ , Z).
In the 70’s Nikulin developed a theory of primitive embeddings of lattices,
generalizing results of Milnor et al. to the non unimodular case. Nikulin’s results
offer criteria for existence and unicity of such embeddings.
These results are very useful for the class of K3 surfaces, of which resolutions
of nodal quartic surfaces are a special case.

10

CONTENTS

If we fix a ‘potential’ K3 code K′ , it turns out, using the results of Nikulin
on integral quadratic forms that there is at most one primitive embedding of
Lsat := Λ ∩ QL inside Λ. Some delicate calculations show that the existence of
such an embedding depends on the congruence class of d modulo 16, while the
potential K3 codes depend only on the congruence class of d modulo 8.
In this way the theorem for quartics extends also to all nodal K3 surfaces as
follows (see Theorem 86 for fuller details and a complete classification).
Theorem 9. The connected components of the Nodal Severi variety FK3 (d, ν)
of nodal K3’ s of degree d (d must be an even number) and with ν nodes are in
bijection with the isomorphism classes of their extended codes K′ (equivalently, of
the pair of codes K ⊂ K′′ ⊂ Fν2 ).
The weights t of vectors in K′′ \ K satisfy the following congruence:
2t − d ≡ 0 (mod 8).
Apart from sporadic cases, which are however obtained via the projection from
a node of a nodal K3 surfaces, we have several main stream cases:
(0) If d ≡ 0 (mod 8) then t ∈ {4, 8, 12} and we get all the shortenings K′′ of
the K8 code (generated by the strict Kummer code KKum and by the characteristic
function of an affine plane π).
(2) If d ≡ 2 (mod 8) and t ∈ {5, 9}, we get all the shortenings K′′ of the code
generated by the linear functions on F42 and by the quadratic function x1 y1 +x2 y2 +1.
(4-6) If d ≡ 4 (mod 8) and t ∈ {6, 10}, we get for K′′ all the shortenings of the
′′
extended Kummer code KKum
.
(4-4) If d ≡ 4 (mod 8) and there is some t = 2 we get all the shortenings of
the special code K′ occurring for ν = 14, k ′ = 4.
(6) If d ≡ 6 (mod 8) we get all the shortenings K′ of the two codes occurring
for ν = 15: the first K′ is the strict Kummer code, in the second K′ there is a
weight 4 vector.
In part I we also give a complete list of all the codes K′ (K′′ ) which occur,
making the above theorem suitable for applications.
An interesting consequence is the following (see Theorem 87)
Theorem 10. Let Y be a nodal K3 surface with ν nodes. Then, for ν ≤ 15,
the fundamental group π1 (Y ∗ ) of the smooth locus of Y is isomorphic to the strict
code K ∼
= (Z/2)k associated to Y .
For ν = 16 Y is a Kummer surface and π1 (Y ∗ ) is the semidirect product
4
Z ⋊ Z/2.
In the third part of the paper we proceed to study the Nodal Severi varieties
F(d, ν) for surfaces of degree d = 5. In this degree the possible weights can only

INTRODUCTION

11

be 16, 20, and this fact was one basic tool which allowed Beauville in his seminal
paper [Bea80] to show that µ(5) = 31, and that if ν = 31, then K is the biduality
code of K ∼
= F52 .
His first crucial observation consisted in what we call here the B-inequalities=
Basic inequalities = Beauville inequalities, which are the lower bounds k ≥
ν − 12 b2 (d), k ′ ≥ ν + 1 − 12 b2 (d), see proposition 32).
Using a result of [Cat81], namely that every quintic surface with ν nodes
and with an even set of nodes of cardinality 16 is the discriminant of a cubic
hypersurface X ⊂ P5 with ν − 16 nodes, for the projection onto P3 with centre a
line L ⊂ X ∗ (contained in the smooth locus of X, as we show here), Beauville was
able to show that every quintic with 31 nodes is a Togliatti quintic, that is, it is
the discriminant of a cubic hypersurface X ⊂ P5 with 15 nodes.
We prove here a new result which is not unexpected, and actually we have
believed for long time to hold true: both Togliatti quintics (quintic surfaces with
31 nodes) and Togliatti hypercubics (cubic hypersurfaces X ⊂ P5 with 15 nodes)
form respective irreducible families.
Again this result is based also on similar ingredients to the previous one on
quartics, in particular it relies on the Torelli theorem for K3 surfaces, this time
applied to complete intersections of type (2, 3) inside P4 .
We actually prove more:
Theorem 11. The Nodal Severi varieties F2 (5, 31) and F4 (3, 15) are irreducible.
For ν ≥ 29 the weight 20 does not appear in the code K and the correspondence
given by taking discriminants of cubic hypersurfaces yields a bijection between the
respective irreducible components of F2 (5, ν) and F4 (3, ν − 16).
These are listed in Subsection 1.7.1.
In the Nodal Severi variety F2 (5, 28) there is exactly one irreducible component
which corresponds to the case where K contains two codewords of weight 20.
All the codes K of nodal quintic surfaces are just:
(1) either the shortenings of the biduality code (simplex code), coming from
smoothings of the Togliatti quintics, or
(2) the shortenings of the unique 2-dimensional code with two codewords of
weight 20,
(3) possibly the unique 2-dimensional code with (exactly) two codewords of
weight 16.
Moreover, F2 (5, 20) contains as irreducible component the variety of quintic
symmetroids (dim(K) = 1, with the weight 20), and at least two other components
(correponding to shortenings of the simplex code, one with dim(K) = 1 and the
weight 16, the other with dim(K) = 0).
Appendix B completely classifies all the codes with weights 16, 20, and those
among them which satisfy the B-inequality (a necessary condition for the codes of

12

CONTENTS

nodal surfaces), hence the codes which could potentially be codes of some nodal
quintic surface.
Our main result is that all these do actually occur as codes of some quintic
surface, with one exception (third in the list) and the possible exception of one
case (sixth in the list) which we cannot resolve, leaving therefore an interesting
open question.
The fourth part of the paper, and most of the second part, devoted to cubic
hypersurfaces and their discriminants, are concerned with our initial aim: the
study of the codes of nodal sextics, especially of those with 65 nodes, the maximum
allowed.
The main underlying idea was that the method of discriminants of cubic hypersurfaces should also work for d = 6, and we show that this is the case, even if
in a rather subtle way.
Recall that Barth constructed [Bar96] a sextic with 65 nodes, and admitting
an icosahedral symmetry; we show here, by the way, that the Barth sextic admits
as group of automorphisms exactly the Icosahedral group (Z/2) × A5 .
′
Our research began with describing first the codes KB , KB
of the Barth sextic,
in an explicit way. Later on Sascha Kurz was able to show, (see appendix C)
via extensive computer calculations (using that the weights of K lie in the set
{24, 32, 40, 56}, and that K can be extended to K′ ) that every sextic surface with
65 nodes has the same extended code as the Barth sextic.
Using the partial classification of half-even sets of nodes on sextics that we
achieve here in subsection 4.13, the knowledge of the code for ν = 65, and the
study of discriminants performed in some sections of the paper (parts 2 and 4),
we are able to show the following theorem:
Theorem 12. Every sextic Y with 65 nodes is the discriminant of a cubic
hypersurface X ⊂ P6 with 31 nodes for the projection of X onto P3 with centre a
plane L intersecting Sing(X) in 3 nodes.
Y can be also realized as the discriminant of a cubic hypersurface X ⊂ P6
with 32 nodes for the projection of X onto P3 with centre a plane L intersecting
Sing(X) in 2 nodes.
The subtle and quite interesting point is that, unlike the case of quintics, where
we project using Togliatti cubics, those with the maximal number of nodes, we
cannot use here the Segre cubic XS ⊂ P6 , which has the maximal number (= 35) of
nodes: since all planes contained in XS are subsets of a three dimensional subspace
contained in XS .
We also show that the Segre cubic XS is locally rigid (up to projective equivalence) in the variety F5 (3, 35).
3A similar classification of cardinalities of even sets of nodes on surfaces of degree 7 could

pave the way to determining µ(7), but the enterprise looks not easy to fulfill.

INTRODUCTION

13

We conjecture that every cubic hypersurface X ⊂ P6 with 35 nodes is projectively equivalent to XS , and we pose similar questions, for instance regarding the
Segre cubic hypersurfaces in projective spaces of even dimension.
The crucial question about the irreducibility of F2 (6, 65) motivates the analogous question about the irreducibility of F5 (3, 32).
Unlike the case of nodal quartics and quintics, where the binary codes occurring
are easy to describe in terms of elementary linear algebra, for the case of the
′
Barth sextic (the case of 65 nodal sextics), the description of the codes KB , KB
is rather intriguing. As discovered by Michael Kiermaier after Yonghwa Cho’s
explicit description of these codes, these are associated to the Doro-Hall graph,
which we briefly describe now.
Theorem 13. The code KB of the Barth sextic YB is associated to the following
graph Γ, whose vertices are the nodes of YB , and where an edge joins two vertices
P, P ′ if and only if the line P P ′ intersects YB in 4 distinct points.
For each vertex P , there are exactly 10 vertices at distance 1, 30 at distance 2,
and 24 at distance 3. The code KB is generated by the characteristic functions of
the 3-spheres of Γ (sets of points at distance 3 from one vertex).
The graph Γ above turns out to be the so-called Doro-Hall graph, whose group
of automorphisms is equal to the group of collineations
PΣL(2, 25) := PSL(2, F25 ) ⋊ (Z/2)
(the non-normal subgroup is the one generated by the Frobenius automorphism of
F25 , yielding a semi-projectivity).
Doro was the first to construct this graph Γ, whose set of vertices is the conjugacy class of the Frobenius automorphism, with an edge connecting two elements
if and only if they commute. Later Hall [Hal80] showed that this was the largest
of the class of the so-called locally Petersen graphs.
Since the Petersen graph is the graph associated to the vertices of the projective dodecahedron (quotient of the dodecahedron by the antipodal map), and
the dodecahedron is the dual polyhedron to the icosahedron, it is natural to ask
whether it is possible to find a direct explanation, starting from the explicit equation of the Barth surface exhibiting its A5 -symmetry, of the fact that the code KB
is the Doro-Hall code associated to the graph Γ as we explained above 4.
The scripts supporting the computer assisted proofs are available at the arXivwebpage of the manuscipt, see [CCFK22].

Generalities and nodal K3 surfaces

1.1. Coding theory notation and basics
i) Given a field F, an F-linear code is an F-vector subspace K of the vector
space FS of the F-valued functions on a given set S; the dimension of
K shall be denoted by k.
Two such codes K1 , K2 are equivalent if there is a bijection S1 ∼
= S2
between the two sets carrying K1 to K2 .
We have a binary code if F = F2 := Z/2.
We shall for simplicity restrict to this case.
It goes without saying that for a binary code any function is the characteristic function χA of a set A, and addition corresponds to the symmetric difference
(A \ B) ∪ (B \ A) = (A ∪ B) \ (A ∩ B).
ii) A bicoloured F-linear code is an F-vector subspace K′ of the vector space
FA∪B , where A ∪ B is a disjoint union. Two such codes K1′ , K2′ are equivalent if there are bijections A1 ∼
= A2 , B1 ∼
= B2 carrying K1′ to K2′ .
To K′ we associate the code K := K′ ∩ FA .
In this paper B shall consist of a single element H (which we identify
to its characteristic function), and, since we shall make the assumption
that H ∈
/ K′ , the bicoloured code K′ will be determined by a pair of codes
K ⊂ K′′ ⊂ FA , where K is as above and K′′ is the projection of K′ into
FA (see item iii) below).
In particular k ′ := dim(K′ ) = dim(K′′ ).
iii) Given a code K ⊂ FS , and N ⊂ S, the shortening KN of K with respect
to N is just the intersection K ∩ FN (KN is just the subspace of the
functions in K with support in N ).
The projection of K determined by N is just the image of K via the
restriction map pN : FS → FN .
Hence the shortening with respect to N is just the kernel of the projection determined by S \ N .
15

16

1. GENERALITIES AND NODAL K3 SURFACES

iv) The effective length n of K is just the cardinality of the union S ′ ⊂ S of
the supports of all the functions in K. K is said to be a spanning code
if n = ν, where ν := |S| is called the length of the code.
v) For a vector v ∈ K, the weight w(v) (also called Hamming weight) is just
the cardinality of the support of the function v. The minimum weight
w(K) is the minimum of the weights of non-zero vectors v ∈ K.
weight enumerator function of K is the function wK (x, y) =
P Then−i
y i , where n is the effective length and ai is the number of vectors
i ai x
of K with weight equal to i.
vi) Dual to the injection K ⊂ FS we have a surjection ϕ : FS → K∨ . The dual
code of K is just ker(ϕ), that is, the perpendicular K⊥ ⊂ FS to K. Given
a subset N ⊂ S, duality exchanges the projection with the shortening:
i.e.,
(pN (K))⊥ = (K⊥ )N .
K is said to be a projective code if the dual code admits no vector of
weight equal to 2.
∨
vii) The biduality code is just the natural embedding K → FK \{0} . Its dual
code is called the Hamming code, and the biduality code is also called
the simplex code (some texts use the historically correct but cumbersome
term: ‘dual Hamming code’).
viii) The McWilliams identity determines the weight enumerator of the dual
code:
1
(1)
wK⊥ (x, y) = k wK (x + y, x − y).

being:

x) Assume that we have a code K where all the weights are just 2m , and
let k = dim(K), n be the effective length. In this case we get from the
McWilliams identity:
2m (2k − 1) = 2k−1 n ⇒ n = (2k − 1)2(m−k+1) ⇒ k ≤ m + 1.
If k = m+1, we have the simplex code, in general a subcode of the simplex
code.
xi) Bonisoli [Bon83] showed that each code with only one weight is gotten
from the simplex code via a product structure: that is, K ⊂ FX×Y

1.1.1. Codes and Radon duality on finite projective spaces. Consider
now more generally a linear code over the finite field Fq , that is, K is a vector
subspace of Fνq of dimension k and which is spanning (full), that is, it has effective
length n = ν.
Let P be the set of points of the projective space Pk−1
Fq .
Up to a permutation automorphism of Fnq , such a spanning Fq -linear code K
is determined by a multiset of n points in P, given by the n rows of a generator
matrix G (a multiset is a map C : P → Z≥0 ): because no such row can be zero,
else K is not spanning.
Two multisets yield the same code if and only if they are projectively equivalent
(this amounts to a change of basis for K). Moreover, since K has dimension k, the
multiset, that is, the support of the function C, must spanP
P.
For A ⊆ P we define the counting function C(A) := x∈A C(x), which shall
also be called the multiplicity of the set A.
To a codeword v = Gx corresponds the hyperplane Hx := x⊥ , and the weight
w(v) of the codeword equals C(P \ Hx ) = n − C(Hx ). And two codewords lead
to the same hyperplane if and only if the corresponding vectors x yield the same
point of P.
Before we show that the weights of code vectors determine the isomorphism
class of a spanning code, we recall a known definition, introduced by Bolker, see
for instance [Bol87], [Kun86].
∨
Definition 14. The function on the dual projective
P space P (whose elements
are the hyperplanes H ⊂ P) defined by C(H) := x∈H C(x) shall be called the
Radon transform R(C) of the function C(x).

The Radon transform R(C) determines the function C(x) by what we call Radon
duality.

1.2. Normal surfaces in P3 , and binary codes of nodal surfaces
Let Y ⊂ P3 := P3C be a normal surface, with singular set Sing(Y ) = {P1 , . . . , Pν }.
Set Y ∗ := Y \Sing(Y ): the topological structure of Y ∗ is an important invariant
of Y , which is not changed by equisingular deformations; in particular the fundamental group π1 (Y ∗ ) and its Abelianization, the first homology group H1 (Y ∗ , Z),
are two basic such invariants.
Given the singular point Pi , let (see [Mil68]) Ui be a neighbourhood of Pi
obtained by intersecting Y with a small Euclidean ball with centre Pi , and set
Ui∗ := Ui \ {Pi }; let further Fi be the Milnor fibre of the singularity (Y, Pi ), and
let Γi be the local fundamental group π1 (Ui∗ ).
Proposition 17. Let Y ⊂ P3 be a normal surface and let Y ∗ be its smooth
locus: then π1 (Y ∗ ) is normally generated by a quotient of the free product Γ1 ∗ Γ2 ∗
· · · ∗ Γν of the local fundamental groups at the singular points.
In particular, H1 (Y ∗ , Z) is a quotient of the direct product of the Abelianizations
of the Γi ’s, and we have a surjection:
ab
ab
∗
Γab
1 × Γ2 × · · · × Γν ↠ H1 (Y , Z).

Proof. We observe that Y deforms to a smooth hypersurface Y ′ , which has
π1 (Y ′ ) = 1 by Lefschetz’ theorem.
Define F to be the union of the Milnor fibres Fi , together with a tree connecting
the base point to each Milnor fibre with a respective segment (in particular π1 (F ) =
1 by Milnor’s theorem [Mil68] stating that Fi is homotopically equivalent to a
bouquet of spheres).
The first van Kampen ’s theorem says that 1 = π1 (Y ′ ) is the quotient of the
free product π1 (Y ∗ ) ∗ π1 (F ) = π1 (Y ∗ ) by the subgroup normally generated by
π1 (F ∗ ), where F ∗ := F ∩ Y ∗ . Now, by construction, π1 (F ∗ ) is the free product
Γ1 ∗ Γ2 ∗ · · · ∗ Γν , hence the desired assertion follows.
When we abelianize, we obtain a surjection of Γ1 ∗ Γ2 ∗ · · · ∗ Γν onto H1 (Y ∗ , Z),
ab
ab
∗
which therefore factors through a surjection Γab
1 × Γ2 × · · · × Γν ↠ H1 (Y , Z). □
We are especially interested in the simplest possible case, where Y is a nodal
surface, that is, all singular points of Y are nodes, singularities of multiplicity
two with nondegenerate Hessian (hence locally biholomorphic to the singularity
y1 y2 − y32 = 0 inside C3 ).
Corollary 18. If Y is a nodal surface in P3 , with Sing(Y ) = {P1 , . . . , Pν },
then H1 (Y ∗ , Z) ∼
= (Z/2)k , and there is a natural surjection ⊕ν1 (Z/2) ↠ H1 (Y ∗ , Z).
Proof. A node is the quotient C2 /(±1), since, if u1 , u2 are local coordinates
on C2 , the quotient is embedded by yi := u2i , i = 1, 2 and by y3 := u1 u2 .
In particular, the local fundamental group is then Z/2.
□

1.2.1. Global orbifold abelian covering.
Remark 19. Proposition 17 and Corollary 18 (and other results below) hold
more generally for:
i) Y a normal surface whose singularities are rational double points (these have
local homology groups Γab
i which are finite) and
ii) whose minimal resolution S is simply connected (since Y deforms differentiably to a smooth Y ′ , which is diffeomorphic to S, see for instance [Cat09]).
For such surfaces then H1 (Y ∗ , Z) is finite.
Definition 20. Let Y be a normal surface such that, setting Y ∗ = Y \Sing(Y ),
|H1 (Y ∗ , Z)| < ∞.
Then there is a finite Galois covering Z → Y , with Z normal, associated to
the surjection π1 (Y ∗ ) → H1 (Y ∗ , Z), and with group G := H1 (Y ∗ , Z).
Z is called the Global (orbifold) abelian covering of Y .
The Galois group being abelian, we have a splitting
M
p∗ (OZ ) =
Fχ ,
χ∈G∗

where, for χ a character of G, the eigensheaf Fχ is torsion free of generic rank 1
and indeed reflexive.
The Global abelian covering has irregularity q(Z) := h1 (OZ ) = 0 if and only if
q(Y ) = 0 and h1 (Fχ ) = 0 for all characters χ.
Moreover, if Y has quotient singularities, Z is smooth if and only if the local
fundamental group Γi injects into G. This criterion is particularly interesting for
the case of singularities which are nodes: Z is smooth if and only if Γi ∼
= Z/2
∗
injects into H1 (Y , Z). Dually, if and only if the dual map is surjective.
A geometric consequence of corollary 18 is that every connected finite Galois
covering Z → Y , ramified only on the singular set of Y , and with abelian Galois
group G, has G = (Z/2)r . Understanding the Galois theory of such covers reduces
to the study of a binary code, defined as follows.

Example 25. (The quadric cone) For d = 2 there is only one nodal surface,
the quadric cone Y := {y1 y2 − y32 = 0}.
Since a curve E with E 2 = −2 is not divisible by 2, it follows that K = 0, while
we claim that K′ is spanned by E + H.
Y is in fact the quotient of P2 , with coordinates (u0 , u1 , u2 ), by the action of
Z/2 sending (u0 , u1 , u2 ) 7→ (u0 , −u1 , −u2 ). The quotient is embedded by

double covering of Ỹ isomorphic to the blow up of P2 at the point u1 = u2 = 0,
and ramified exactly on E ∪ H.
Hence the divisor E + H is divisible by 2, and yields a class in K′ .
Also, π1 (Y ∗ \ H) ∼
= Z/2 and P2 is the Global abelian covering of Y .
We have again another characterization for the extended code.

the two properties that its minimal S = Ỹ is simply connected, and that the class
of H is indivisible; it suffices to observe that the only facts that we have used are
that Pic(Ỹ ) is a free abelian group, because Ỹ is simply connected, and that the
class of H is indivisible.
□
Remark 27. In particular, the same result holds also for a nodal K3 surface
Y , with minimal resolution S, such that class of the hyperplane divisor H on S is
indivisible.
1.2.2. Examples.
Example 28. (A cubic with maximal Milnor number)
A similar example to Example 25 is the quotient P2 /(Z/3) for the action such
that (u0 , u1 , u2 ) 7→ (u0 , ϵu1 , ϵ2 u2 ), where ϵ is a primitive third root of unity.
The quotient is embedded by xi := u3i , i = 0, 1, 2 and by y3 := u0 u1 u2 , so that
P2 /(Z/3) ∼
= Y := {x0 x1 x2 = x3 }.
3

In this case the triple covering is only ramified in the three singular points
y3 = yi = yj = 0 ( for 0 ≤ i < j ≤ 2), hence we conclude that (since P2 minus
three points is simply connected)
π1 (Y ∗ ) ∼
= Z/3,
and again we have a Global abelian covering.
This cubic surface is remarkable, since it has 3 singular points with Milnor
number 2 (locally isomorphic to the singularity x1 x2 = x33 ), and 3 · 2 = 6 is bigger
than the maximum number of singular points that a normal cubic can have, which
equals to 4.
For each normal cubic surface Y , which is not the cone over a smooth cubic
curve, its singularities are rational double points, hence the sum m := M ilnor(Y )
of the Milnor numbers of the singular points equals the number of the exceptional
(−2)-curves appearing in the resolution Ỹ ; since Ỹ is the blow up of the plane
in 6 points, the rank of H 2 (Ỹ , Z) is at most 7, hence (these (−2)-curves being

1.2. NORMAL SURFACES IN P3 , BINARY CODES OF NODAL SURFACES

25

numerically independent, see [Art66]) M ilnor(3) ≤ 6, and equality is attained in
this example.
Example 29. (The Cayley cubic) A normal cubic can have at most 4 singular
points, and if it does have 4 singular points, then it is projectively equivalent to
the Cayley cubic Y := {σ3 (x0 , x1 , x2 , x3 ) = 0}, where σ3 is the third elementary
symmetric function (the four singular points are the 4 coordinate points).
We give another proof of the following known theorem (see [DFTTA09], and
Theorem 34 of [Cat21] which follows the same idea but contains more explicit
details)
Theorem 30. If Y is the Cayley cubic, then
π1 (Y ∗ ) ∼
= Z/2.
Proof. In fact, Y admits a double covering Z ramified only in the four singular
points: this can be seen by representing Y as the determinant of a symmetric
matrix of linear forms, or as a consequence of the B-inequality of Proposition 32
which shows that the code K of the Cayley cubic has dimension 1 and is generated
by the vector with 4 coordinates equal to 1.
Z is a smooth Del Pezzo surface of degree 6, hence isomorphic to the blow up
of P2 in three points. Since Z minus a finite number of points is simply connected,
follows that π1 (Y ∗ ) ∼
□
= Z/2, and Z is the Global abelian covering.
Instead for other nodal cubics the associated code is trivial, because (see
[Cat81]) the number of nonzero coordinates of a vector in K is always divisible
by 4, and divisible by 8 if the degree of the nodal surface is even.
We have more (see Proposition 35 of [Cat21] for a simple proof):
Proposition 31. If Y is a nodal cubic with ν ≤ 3 nodes, then Y ∗ is simply
connected.

1.3. Code shortenings and unobstructed nodal surfaces
Definition 34. 1) Consider a linear code K ⊂ FS , and a subset N ⊂ S.
Then the shortening of the code K relative to the subset N , denoted by
Res(K, N ), or more briefly by KN , was defined as
KN := Res(K, N ) := {f |f ∈ K, supp(f ) ⊂ N } ⊂ FN .
In case of an extended code K′ ⊂ FS ⊕ FH, we denote by shorthand notation
′
KN
= Res(K′ , N ) := Res(K′ , N ∪ H).

2) A binary code K ⊂ FS2 is said to be d-realized if K is the code of a nodal
surface of degree d in P3 .
Definition 35. If Y is a nodal hypersurface of degree d in Pn , Y is said to
be unobstructed if its singular points impose linearly independent conditions on
the vector space of homogeneous polynomials of degree d.
The geometrical meaning of unobstructedness is that the deformations of Y
have a submersion onto the space of local deformations of the singularities, so that
one can obtain independent smoothings of all the singular points.

neighbourhood of Y we can find all possible local deformations of the singularities
of Y .

Theorem 36. Let Y be an unobstructed nodal surface of degree d: then all

they are realized by some nodal surface of degree d.

of the union of all the curves Ei , for i ∈ S.

iii) In general the singular points of the Segre cubic are the points where the
gradients of s1 , s3 are proportional, hence x2i = x2j for all pairs i, j. The condition
s1 (x) = 0, implies that half of the xi are equal to 1, the other half to −1. For P5
we get 10 singular points, corresponding to the partitions of {0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5} into

Consider the cubic polynomials of the form (xi +xj )(xa +xb )(xc −xd ) where the
6 indices are all different. Such a polynomial vanishes on a singular point unless
xi = xj , xa = xb whereas xc and xd have opposite sign.
So, it vanishes for all but 2 singular points, for instance (1, 1, −1, −1, 1, −1)
and (1, 1, −1, −1, −1, 1) if the indices are in increasing order 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6.
However, for any two such different singular points, since we are in projective
space, they have four equal coordinates, two of them = 1, the other two = −1,
and two opposite coordinates.
Hence there is a polynomial as above taking in both points the same value ±8,
and vanishing in the others. Hence we see that the image of the evaluation map
contains a basis of C10 .

1.3.1. An instructive extended code: the case of the Beniamino Segre
surfaces. We want here to give a simple illustration of the application of coding
theory to geometry, giving a characterization of the Segre surfaces of Example 37
in low degree.
These have equation
Y := {x|λ1 (x) . . . λd (x) − B(x)2 = 0},
where B(x) is a form of degree m = 12 d, and the points λi (x) = λj (x) = B(x) = 0,
varying 1 ≤ i < j ≤ d, are nodes.
The equation can be seen in many ways, for each permutation of {1, . . . , d}
and for all r < d, as the determinant of a symmetric 2 × 2 matrix of forms,
F (x) = detA(x), where


As we see from [Cat81], such a determinantal expression produces an even set of
nodes with cardinality mr(d − r), and which is 12 -even for r odd, and strictly even
for r even.
For r = 1 we obtain a codeword wi of K′′ with weight m(d−1) = 12 d(d−1), and
it is easy to see that these vectors (codewords) generate a subcode KS′′ of dimension
d − 1, since the sum w1 + · · · + wd = 0 of these vectors is zero; we shall call this
code the Segre code.
This code is derived from the 2-partition code C(2, Σ) on the set Σ := {1, . . . , d},
which is defined as follows.
Let M be the family of subsets D of Σ with 2 elements, and associate to a
2-partition of Σ (Σ is the disjoint union A∪B), the function ϕA,B such ϕA,B (D) = 1
if and only if D contains an element of A and an element of B (these functions
shall be called 2-partition functions).
Then
C(2, Σ) ⊂ FM
2 , C(2, Σ) := {ϕA,B |Σ = A ∪ B, A ∩ B = ∅.}
C(2, Σ) has 2d−1 elements, equal to 12 the cardinality of the power set P(Σ), hence
C(2, Σ) has dimension (d − 1) 1. The sum of elements ϕA,B + ϕA′ ,B′ corresponds to
the symmetric difference A∆A′ , that is, to the 2-partition ((A ∩ A′ ) ∪ (B ∩ B ′ )) ∪
((A ∩ B ′ ) ∪ (A′ ∩ B)).
We see that KS′′ is an m-times repeated C(2, Σ) code, i.e. obtained via the
product structure N := M × {1, . . . , m}.

We have then the following characterization
Theorem 42. Let Y be a nodal surface of degree d = 2m in P3 , and assume
that the code K′′ admits a shortening which is (isomorphic to) the Segre code KS′′
in degree d, i.e. KS′′ is an m-times repeated C(2, Σ) code.
Then, for d ≤ 8, Y admits an equation of the form
Y := {x|λ1 (x) . . . λd (x) − B(x)2 = 0},
where the λi (x) are linear forms.
Proof. Let Ni be the 21 -even set corresponding to the codeword wi , so that
|Ni | = m(d − 1): we have a linear equivalence on the minimal resolution S of Y ,

EP + H.

Using Theorem 1.10, part (ii) of Endrass [End98], we infer that Li is effective

P there is a linear form λi (x) such that its divisor on S is of the
form 2Ci + P ∈Ni EP , where Ci contains no exceptional divisor (the multiplicity
of a linear form at a node being either 1 or 0).
Consider now the product Λ(x) := λ1 (x) . . . λd (x). Its divisor on S is an
effective divisor of the form 2D, because the sum of the vectors wi equals zero.
Then, since there is no torsion in Pic(S), D is cut by a form B(x) of degree
m = 21 d, and we have that λ1 (x) . . . λd (x) − B(x)2 = 0 is zero on Y . But it is
not identically zero because the linear forms are distinct, hence we obtained the
equation of Y .
□
Remark 43. It was conjectured by Endrass [End98] on the basis of a result
by Gallarati [Gal60] that for half-even sets of nodes N the cardinality is always at
least ≥ 21 d(d − 1), equality holding if and only if N is symmetric and L is effective,
that is, it corresponds to writing the equation F of Y as λ(x)ϕ(x) − B(x)2 , with
λ(x) a linear form.
If the conjecture is true, then the above characterization of the B. Segre surfaces
of Example 37 holds for all (even) degrees.
1.4. Codes and ‘fundamental groups’ of nodal quartic surfaces
A nodal quartic surface can have at most 16 nodes.
This follows from the cruder estimate that the dual surface Y ∨ of a nodal
surface Y with ν nodes has degree δ = d(d − 1)2 − 2ν, and the observation that
the degree δ of Y ∨ is at least d (for d ≥ 4 the argument is that, since Y and Y ∨
are birational, they have the same geometric genus, while, for d ≤ 3, this follows
by biduality (Y ∨ )∨ = Y ).
Hence, for d = 3, we get ν ≤ 4, as we already discussed, while for d = 4 ν ≤ 16,
equality holding if and only if Y ∨ is also a quartic surface.

34

1. GENERALITIES AND NODAL K3 SURFACES

We shall now discuss the well known fact that if ν = 16, then Y is the quotient
of a principally polarized Abelian surface A by the group {±1}, embedded by the
theta functions of second order on A (see for instance [Cat21], which shows also
that, in suitable coordinates, Y = Y ∨ ).
This can be shown in several ways, but we choose here the coding theoretic
approach, pioneered by Nikulin [Nik75]: the main idea is that, knowing that the
strict code K associated to a nodal quartic has weights only 8 or 16, there exists a
codeword of weight 16, hence a double covering ramified exactly at the 16 nodes.
We begin with a rather elementary result in coding theory, for which we give a
detailed proof as a preparation for other precise results (a shorter proof could be
obtained invoking Bonisoli’s theorem [Bon83]).
Proposition 44. Let K ⊂ Fν2 be a code with ν ≤ 16 and weights only 8, 16.
Then K is contained in the Kummer code KKum := Af f (F42 , F2 ), a first order
F4
Reed-Muller code, consisting of the vector space of affine functions inside F2 2 .
Moreover, if the weights are never equal to 16, it is a shortening of the Kummer
code.
Proof. We observe that K is contained in F16
2 , where there is a unique vector
e of weight 16.
We take V := K if K contains e, otherwise we let V be the span of F2 e and of
K.
Notice that e corresponds to the constant function 1.
If there is another non

That V has at most dimension 5 follows from a similar argument, or from the
Mac Williams identities (2) applied to a supplement W of F2 e inside V .
In fact we have then, if (W ) is a Chn code, that is, a code of effective length n
and dimension h, and a8 is the number of vectors of weight 8,
a8 = 2h − 1, 8a8 = n2k−1 ⇒ n = (2h − 1)24−h ,
implying that h ≤ 4.
We construct now a bijection of {1, . . . , 16} with F42 , by using the functions
v1 , v2 , v3 , v4 (we identify ei with its characteristic function).
Hence it is proven that K is contained in the Kummer code.
For the second assertion, observe that, if K does not contain e, there are the
four cases, where by a permutation of the coordinates we may assume that a basis
of K is given by v1 , . . . , vk .
For k = 1 we get the shortening associated to any subset of {1, 2, . . . , 11} which
contains the subset {1, 2, . . . , 8}, for k = 2 the shortening associated to the subsets
{1, 2, . . . , 12} and {1, 2, . . . , 13}, for k = 3 the shortening associated to the subset
{1, 2, . . . , 14} and for k = 4 the shortening associated to the subset {1, 2, . . . , 15}.
Indeed, when we take the shortening of the Kummer code by removing one
point in affine space, this point may be chosen as the origin in a vector space, and
we have the 4-dimensional space K of linear functions. Removing one, respectively
two vectors, the dimension of K drops to 3, respectively 2. Removing a third
vector, there are two cases, depending on whether the three vectors are linearly
independent or not; the dimension drops to 1, respectively 2.
Once we remove at least four vectors plus the origin, three of them are independent and generate a 3-dimensional linear subspace U , and if the dimension of
the shortening K stays 1, this means that the fourth vector we are removing is
another one of the four remaining nonzero vectors in U .
Finally, the condition that K does not contain e is necessary, since F2 e is not
a shortening of the Kummer code.
□
Remark 45. In fact, as done in [Cat96] for generalized codes of this type, it
is convenient to have the following combinatorial representation of the Kummer
code as a chessboard code.
We represent {1, . . . , 16} ∼
= F42 as W1 × W2 , where Wi := Vi∨ and Vi is a
2-dimensional vector space over F2 .
Then V1 ⊗ V2 is the space of linear functions on W1 × W2 , and the space K
of affine functions on W1 × W2 is represented as the set of functions f such that,
∀i, j,
i) P
f (i, j) = f (0, 0) + f (0, j) + f (i, 0),
ii) j f (i, j) = 0,

36

1. GENERALITIES AND NODAL K3 SURFACES

P

iii) i f (i, j) = 0.
In fact, then, if {e1 , e2 } is a basis for W1 , and {ϵ1 , ϵ2 } is a basis for W2 , then
F := f + f (0, 0) satisfies the three equations
(4)

F (i, j) = F (0, j) + F (i, 0),

(5)

F (0, ϵ1 ) + F (0, ϵ2 ) + F (0, ϵ1 + ϵ2 ) = 0,

(6)

F (e1 , 0) + F (e2 , 0) + F (e1 + e2 , 0) = 0,

hence F is linear.
In this representation, v1 has entries equal to 1 exactly on the first
two rows, v2 on the first and third row, v3 on the first two columns, v4
on the first and third column.

Playing the same game for the other pairs of rows, we conclude that for each
partition into two pairs, one pair gives sum equal to 2, and the other gives sum
equal to 4.
As a consequence, we observe that, if for instance a1 + a2 = 4, then necessarily
a1 ≤ 3, and a2 ≥ 1.
It follows that, for all i, we have ai ≤ 3, else there is a pair with ai + aj ≤ 1.
If there is a j with aj = 3, then all others are odd, and equal to 1; otherwise,
∀i, ai ≤ 2, and since there is a j with aj = 2, we conclude that all the ai ’s are
even.
Hence either the four numbers ai are, after a permutation, (2, 2, 2, 0), or they
are (3, 1, 1, 1).
The same must also be true for the column sums.
To simplify the argument, we use the fact that the group of affinities of Wi
equals the full symmetric group, permuting rows for i = 1 (respectively: columns
for i = 2) and that we can use the symmetries in Af f (W1 ) × Af f (W2 ), and also
exchange rows and columns (this amounts to exchanging W1 with W2 ): we shall
consider two functions equivalent if obtained one from the other via a sequence of
these operations.

(i, j) ∈ W1 × W2 7→ A(i, j) = (i, j + ϕ(i)),
where ϕ : W1 → W2 is an affine map (in particular, we may get any permutation!).
These above transformations preserve the rows, and we have similar ones preserving the columns.
For instance, j 7→ j + e1 transposes the first two columns and the last two ones.

Corollary 49. Let Y be a nodal surface of degree d = 4 in P3 , and with
ν = 16 nodes. Then Y is a Kummer surface, Y = A/ ± 1, where A is a principally
polarized Abelian surface.
In particular, π1 (Y ∗ ) is the semidirect product Z4 ⋊ Z/2 corresponding to multiplication by ±1.
′
, that is, the direct
Its extended code K′ is the extended Kummer code KKum
sum KKum ⊕ F2 β of proposition 46, where β is the bilinear form β := x1 y1 + x2 y2 .
More generally, let Y be a nodal surface of degree d and with ν = 16 nodes.
Then d is divisible by 4, d = 4m, and Y is a Kummer surface, Y = A/ ± 1, where
A is an Abelian surface with a polarization of type (1, m).
Again π1 (Y ∗ ) is the semidirect product Z4 ⋊Z/2 corresponding to multiplication
by ±1.
Its extended code K′ is the extended Kummer code for m odd, and for m even
the space generated by the affine functions and the characteristic function fπ of an
affine plane π.
Proof. By the B-inequality the code K of Y has dimension at least 5, hence
the previous proposition 44 tells that we have exactly the Kummer code, in particular there exists a double covering A → Y branched exactly on the 16 nodes
(and where A is smooth).
We have that KA is a trivial divisor, in particular A is minimal.
By proposition 2.11 of [Cat81] we have that χ(OA ) = 0, hence, by the classification theorem of algebraic surfaces, A is an Abelian surface. Since there is an
involution ι on A which has fixed points, we can assume, up to a translation on
A, that ι is multiplication by ±1.
Finally, the pull back of the hyperplane divisor H on Y is a divisor D with
D2 = 8 and with h0 (A, O(D)) ≥ 4. Hence D is a polarization of type (d1 , d2 ) with
d1 d2 = 4. Since however all the sections of H 0 (A, OA (D)) are even functions, it
follows that d1 = d2 = 2 and D is twice a principal polarization.
Writing A = C2 /Λ, where Λ ∼
= Z4 , Y is the quotient of C2 by the group Γ of
transformations z 7→ ±z + λ, for λ ∈ Λ.

42

Γ is then isomorphic to the semidirect product Z4 ⋊ Z/2 corresponding to
multiplication by ±1.
We calculate its abelianization, set Λ := Z4 and let g be the generator of Z/2:
since g 2 = 1, gλg = −λ, in the abelianization K we have
2[g] = 0, ∀λ ∈ Λ, [λ] = −[λ] ⇔ 2[λ] = 0,
hence K ∼
= (Z/2)5 .
Since C2 \ Λ is simply connected, and Y ∗ = (C2 \ Λ)/Γ, where the action is
free, we obtain that π1 (Y ∗ ) ∼

Take a symmetric theta divisor D, so that D is ι-invariant, and it descends to
a Weil divisor D′ on Y . Observe that D passes exactly through the six odd half
periods, i.e., those for which β(x1 , x2 , y1 , y2 ) equals 1, and with multiplicity one.
The desired divisor L is the proper transform of D′ : and sinceP
2D′ is a hyperplane section, we have the desired linear equivalence H ≡ 2L + i∈N Ei , where

Since, for some h ∈ N, L + hH is effective, for the pull back D of H we have
that (2h + 1)D ≡ 2∆, where the divisor ∆ is effective. Hence D is 2 divisible,
D = 2D′ and D2 is divisible by 8, hence d = H 2 is divisible by 4, d = 4m. And
then D′ is of type (a, b) with ab = m.
Recall that a|b, hence, if a ̸= 1, then D′ = aD′′ and the divisor D = 2aD′′ is
the pull-back of H, and since 2D′′ is the pull back of a Cartier divisor H ′′ on Y ,
we get H = aH ′′ , contradicting that H is indivisible. Hence a = 1, b = m.
The cardinality of the half-even set N (which must be ̸= 0) is the number of
half periods where the divisor D′ has odd multiplicity. We can take D′ the pull
back of a principal polarization under an isogeny with kernel Z/m.
More precisely, we consider the lattice
Λ′ := (Z ⊕ mZ) ⊕ τ Z2 ⊂ Λ := (Z ⊕ Z) ⊕ τ Z2 ⊂ C2 ,
for τ in the Siegel domain, and calculate the Riemann theta function θ(τ, z) at the
half periods.
The group of half periods for Λ′ maps, for m odd, isomorphically to the group
of half periods for Λ, hence for m odd we get the points where the multiplicity is
odd, these correspond to the solutions of β(x1 , x2 , y1 , y2 ) = 1.

While, for m even, x2 maps to 2x2 , hence we get the solutions of x1 y1 = 1:
these are the points of the affine plane x1 = y1 = 1.

We want now to give a first sharpening of a result shown in [CKO03], theorem
B and in [Cam04], asserting the finiteness of the fundamental group of the smooth
locus; we shall extend this result to other nodal K3 surfaces in the sequel (Theorem
87).
Theorem 50. Let Y be a nodal quartic surface in P3 , with ν nodes, where
ν ≤ 15: then the fundamental group π1 (Y ∗ ) of its smooth locus is finite and is
isomorphic to its binary code K, which is in turn determined by its dimension
k, 0 ≤ k ≤ 4.
The subset of the Nodal Severi variety F(4, ν) corresponding to nodal quartic
surfaces with fixed number ν ≤ 16 of nodes and fixed binary code K′ is smooth and
connected, hence irreducible.
The possible codes K′ appearing are in particular all the shortenings of the
extended Kummer code of corollary 49, and they are listed in Section 1.8 (cases
(0), (00), (i), (1), (2) , (II),(III-1), (III-2), (a), (b), (c), (d), hence we have 11
nontrivial codes in their minimal embedding).
The Nodal Severi variety F(4, ν) has exactly one irreducible component for ν =
0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 14, 15, 16, exactly two irreducible components for ν = 6, 7, 13, exactly
three irreducible components for ν = 8, 9, exactly four irreducible components for
ν = 11, 12, exactly five irreducible components for ν = 10. Hence its stratification
is made of 34 strata, where each stratum is in the boundary of another if and
only if the corresponding extended code admits the other as a shortening, as in the
following Genealogy tree = Kummer nontrivial genealogy for nodal quartic surfaces
(Figure 1).

We want now to show that if a potential nodal K3 lattice L′ admits an isometric
embedding into the K3 lattice Λ, then this embedding is unique (up to isometries
of Λ).
To this purpose we shall apply Nikulin’s theorem 1.14.4 of [Nik79], asserting
that under three (sufficient) conditions there exists a unique primitive embedding.
This assertion must be taken with a grain of salt, since what really Nikulin means
is just that there exists at most one such embedding (it is unique, if it exists).
The conditions are:
• 1) Positivity of (Λ) > Positivity of (L′ ), Negativity of (Λ) > Negativity
of (L′ ),
• 2) rank(Λ) ≥ rank(L′ ) + 2 + l(Disc(L′ )p ) for all primes p ̸= 2,
• 3) If rank(Λ) = rank(L′ ) + l(Disc(L′ )2 ), then the quadratic form q of L′
(2)

splits a summand either of the form u+ (2) or of the form v+ (2);
• 4) Condition 3) is automatically verified if Disc(L′ ) ∼
= A′ ⊕ (Z/2)3 (see
remark 1.14.5 of [Nik79]).

The conclusion of the above theorem is that L′ and its embedding in Λ is
uniquely defined by the code K′ and by the integer d.
The next question is when does a potential nodal K3 lattice admit an isometric
embedding into the K3 lattice Λ.
Theorem 1.12.2 of [Nik79] answers this question giving necessary and sufficient
conditions.
These conditions are:
• 1’) Positivity of (Λ) ≥ Positivity of (L′ ), Negativity of (Λ) ≥ Negativity
of (L′ ),
• 2’) rank(Λ) ≥ rank(L′ ) + l(Disc(L′ )),
• 3’) a certain congruence condition for the primes p ≥ 3 such that rank(Λ) =
rank(L′ ) + l(Disc(L′ )p ),
• 4’) a certain congruence condition between 2-adic units (here K(q2 ) is the
2-adic lattice having associated quadratic form q2 ):
|Disc(L′ )| ≡ ±discr(K(q2 )) (mod (Z∗2 )2 ),
which must be verified in the case where
rank(Λ) = rank(L′ ) + l(Disc(L′ )2 ),
(2)

and where moreover q2 does not split off a summand qθ (2).
Remark 57. i) As we shall see, the last condition 4’) will be the only one
which is not always verified, and which, fixed the code K′ , will lead to a distinction depending on the congruence class of d modulo (16). This distinction is

important, since we shall classify the potential nodal K3 codes, and show that

ii) In many cases however we shall show the existence of these embeddings by
purely geometric methods (smoothings or projections).
1.5.1. Proof of Theorem 6 = Theorem 50.
Proof. Consider a Kummer surface Y0 , which is unobstructed (cf. instance
the Duke of Cefalú surface illustrated in Section 1.3): then if Y is a small deformation of Y , then Y ∗ is obtained from Y˜0 by removing 16 − ν (−2)-curves Ei .
We have seen that the fundamental group of Y0∗ is the affine group of transformations of R4
x 7→ ±x + λ, λ ∈ Z4 ,
and it is generated by simple small loops γi around the curves Ei , which correspond
to the symmetry around a half period 21 λi of R4 /Z4 , γi (x) = −x + λi .
Without loss of generality we may assume that the half period 0 corresponds
to one of the 16 − ν (−2)-curves Ei which we stick in again to get Y ∗ from Y0∗ .
Hence the fundamental group of Y ∗ is a quotient of Γ by the normal subgroup
generated by x 7→ −x, and is therefore a quotient of (Z/2)4 , hence it is Abelian,
and equal to H1 (Y ∗ , Z). The latter is in turn isomorphic to K.
That K is determined by its dimension was shown in the course of the proof of
proposition 44.
Clearly ν, K ⊂ K′ , are deformation invariant for equisingular deformations, and
we can take different shortenings of the Kummer code yielding the same number
ν, but different codes: this shows that fixing only ν we get a disconnected set.
On the other hand, the code K′ , as in corollary 26, is shown to be equal to the
cokernel
H1 (Y ∗ \ H, Z) ∼
= coker[H 2 (Ỹ , Z) → ⊕ν1 Z[Ei ] ⊕ ZH].
of the dual map of the embedding inside the 22-dimensional K3 lattice Λ :=
H 2 (Ỹ , Z) of the lattice L generated by the curves Ei and by the hyperplane section
H.
We have seen (ibidem) that the saturation L′ := Lsat := Λ ∩ QL consists of
the vectors

H 2,0 ⊕ H 0,2 , which is orthogonal to H.

the two connected components of D. The proof is then completed if we show that
there is, for each choice of K′ , a real surface Y (Y = Y ).

with coefficients in Z, and also its singular points have coordinates ∈ {0, 1} ⊂ Z;

1.6. Cubic 4-folds and nodal K3 surfaces of degree 6
The present short section gives some motivation for the study of nodal K3
surfaces of degree 6, which shall be useful also for the study of nodal quintic
surfaces.
We begin by recalling a well known elementary result, which is however crucial
for our description
Lemma 58. Let P be a point of multiplicity 2 of a cubic hypersurface X ⊂ PN .
Take coordinates (x, z) := (x0 , . . . , xn , z) where N = n + 1 and P = (0, 1); consider
the Taylor development at P of the equation F of X, F = Q(x)z + G(x).
Define Y ⊂ Pn as the complete intersection Y = {x|Q(x) = G(x) = 0} ⊂ Pn .
Then
i) Sing(X) ⊂ {(x, z)|Q(x) = G(x) = 0} = (P ∗ Y ) : the singular set of X is
contained in the cone over Y with vertex P .
ii) If x ∈ Y , x ∈
/ Sing(Q), then the line L = P ∗ x (contained in X), contains
at most another singular point (x, z) of X (x ̸= 0), and this happens if
and only if x ∈ Sing(Y ).
iii) In the previous situation, x is a node of Y if and only if (x, z) is a node
of X.
iv) If x ∈ Sing(Q) ∩ Y (⊂ Sing(Y )) , then the line L = P ∗ x contains another
singular point of X (different from P ) if and only if L ⊂ Sing(X); this
happens if and only if x ∈ Sing(G).
Proof. i):
{(x, z) ∈ Sing(X)|x ̸= 0} =
= {(x, z)|x ̸= 0, Q(x) = 0, G(x) = 0, z(∂i Q) + ∂i G = 0, ∀i = 0, . . . , n}.
ii): if there is an i such that ∂i Q(x) ̸= 0, then z is uniquely determined, and it
exists if and only if the gradient of G is proportional to the one of Q.
iv): if the gradient of Q vanishes at x, there is another singular point if and
only if also the gradient of G vanishes at x.
iii): observe that we have a node of X if and only if the ideal (Q, z(∂i Q) + ∂i G)
is locally the maximal ideal of the point; but, if ∂0 Q ̸= 0, this ideal is the ideal
(Q, −(∂0 G)(∂i Q) + (∂i G)(∂0 Q)).
We may take Q as a local coordinate y, and then in local coordinates (uj , y)
we get the ideal (y, ∂j G), which is the Jacobian ideal of Y .
□
Corollary 59. X is nodal if and only if
(I) Q is smooth

54

1. GENERALITIES AND NODAL K3 SURFACES

(II) Y is nodal.
Moreover, in this case the number γ of nodes of X equals the number of nodes
ν of Y plus 1.
Conversely, if Y is nodal, then the corank of Q is at most 2, and if the corank
equals 2, then X has ν − 3 nodes plus the other singular point P , which is not a
node.
Proof. For the first assertion, (I) is a necessary condition, since P is a node
and Q is the quadratic part of F at P . If Q is smooth, then the equivalence of X
and Y being nodal follows from iii) of Lemma 58.
For the second assertion, since Y has isolated singularities, Sing(Q) has projective dimension at most 1, and if equality holds, then G intersects this singular
line in exactly 3 distinct points, which are nodes for Y .
□
1.7. Nodal K3 surfaces of arbitrary degree.
The following proposition motivates the definition, given earlier, of potential
nodal K3 codes K′ .
Proposition 60. Let Y be
• a nodal K3 surface in P4 (a complete intersection of type (2, 3)), respectively
• a nodal K3 surface of degree d = 4m + 2 (where the ample divisor H is
indivisible).
Then the number ν of nodes of Y is at most 15, the weights of the code K are
equal to 8, while
• the weights of K′ are divisible by 4 in the first case, respectively
• the weights of K′ are divisible by 4 for m odd, and congruent to 2 modulo
4 if m is even.
Moreover, dim(K) ≥ dim(K′ ) − 1, dim(K′ ) ≥ ν − 10 (hence dim(K) ≥ ν − 11).
Finally, if Y is a nodal K3 surface of degree d = 4m, the weights of the code K
are either 8 or 16, while the weights of vectors in K′ \K, when written as w = t+1,
satisfy t ≡ 2m mod(4) (that is, for m odd t ≡ 2 mod(4), for m even t ≡ 0 mod(4)).
Proof. The
Pdetermination of the weights follows from the theorem of Riemann
Roch: if 2L = t1 Ei + δH on the resolution of singularities of Y , with δ ∈ {0, 1},
then χ(−L) = χ(O) + 21 L2 , hence L2 is an even number.
But 4L2 = −2t+dδ hence 4 divides d2 δ −t. For δ = 1 we get that t ≡ d2 mod(4);
for δ = 0 we use the usual fact that the first cohomology group carries a nondegenerate alternating form, hence L2 is divisible by 4 and t is divisible by 8.
For the other assertion, we know in any case that ν ≤ 16: if equality holds,
then by the B-inequalities the dimension of K is equal to 16 − 11 = 5, hence K is
the strict Kummer code and K′ ̸= K. Therefore, for m odd, there is a vector in
K′ with t ≡ 3 (mod 4), and adding the vector in K with all coordinates equal to

1.7. NODAL K3 SURFACES OF ARBITRARY DEGREE.

55

1, we get a vector in K with t′ = 16 − t ≡ 1 (mod 4), a contradiction; similarly for
the case m even.
□
Remark 61. If the degree d is 2, then Y is a finite double cover of the plane
branched on a plane curve B with only nodes as singularities, which correspond
to the nodes of Y .
The codewords in K′ \ K correspond to components ∆ of B of odd degree, with
t equal to the number of nodes where ∆ has odd multiplicity (t = 5 if ∆ is a line,
or has degree 5, t = 9 if ∆ is a cubic).
The codewords in K correspond to divisors ∆ ≤ B of even degree, with t equal
to the number of nodes where ∆ has odd multiplicity.
Then we have an easy picture: the maximum number ν = 15 is obtained when
B consists of 6 lines intersecting in 15 points (and we get the projection of a
Kummer surface in P3 if there is a smooth conic tangent to all the six lines in
points different from the 15 nodes).
In this case each line in the branch curve B produces a codeword with weight
6 in K′ , and (t = 5) a codeword of weight 5 in the projected code K′′ , namely
X
vi :=
Ei,j .
i̸=j

The sum of these 6 vectors vi is the zero vector, and these vectors span K′ , which
has dimension 5.
By our above remarks on the weights of vectors in K′ , K′ is contained in the
projection of the extended Kummer code (contained in the second Reed -Muller
code for the affine space F42 ). If we take the hyperplane point H as corresponding
to the origin of the affine space, we get the following description (see Section 1.8).
(d=2) K′ consists of the subspace generated by the linear forms and
the quadratic function q ′ (x, y) := x1 y1 + x2 y2 + 1.
All the other configurations are obtained by smoothings of the curve which is
the union of six lines, and the corresponding codes are shortenings of the above
code.
In view of Proposition 48 we record the conclusion of the above discussion.
Proposition 62. Let K′ ⊂ Fν2 ⊕ F2 H be a bicoloured code such that ν ≤ 15,
such that the weight of vectors of K is 8, and the other weights of K′ are 6 or 10.
Then K′ is a shortening of the subspace of the second order Reed-Muller code
which is generated by the linear forms and the quadratic function q ′ (x, y) := x1 y1 +
x2 y2 + 1.
It is a fun exercise to classify geometrically all the possible cases (we proceed
by smoothing one node at each step). Recall that k ′ := dim(K′ ) ≥ k := dim(K),
and observe that in the following list K′ = 0 if B is irreducible.
i) ν = 14: conic plus 4 lines (k = 3, k ′ = 4)

56

1. GENERALITIES AND NODAL K3 SURFACES

ii) ν = 13: two conics plus 2 lines (k = 2, k ′ = 3) or nodal cubic plus 3 lines
(k = 2, k ′ = 3);
iii) ν = 12: three conics (k = k ′ = 2) or smooth cubic plus 3 lines (k = 2, k ′ =
3) or 3-nodal quartic plus two lines (k = 1, k ′ = 2) or nodal cubic plus
conic plus line (k = 1, k ′ = 2)
iv) ν = 11: two nodal cubics (k = 0, k ′ = 1) or smooth cubic plus conic plus
line (k = 1, k ′ = 2) or 2-nodal quartic plus two lines (k = 1, k ′ = 2) or
6-nodal quintic plus line (k = 0, k ′ = 1) or conic plus three-nodal quartic
(k = k ′ = 1)
v) ν = 10: nodal cubic plus smooth cubic (k = 0, k ′ = 1) or conic plus twonodal quartic (k = k ′ = 1) or 1-nodal quartic plus two lines (k = 1, k ′ = 2)
or irreducible 5-nodal quintic plus line (k = 0, k ′ = 1) or irreducible 10nodal sextic
vi) ν = 9: two smooth cubics (k = 0, k ′ = 1) or conic plus one-nodal quartic
(k = k ′ = 1) or smooth quartic plus two lines (k = 1, k ′ = 2) or irreducible
4-nodal quintic plus line (k = 0, k ′ = 1) or irreducible 9-nodal sextic
vii) ν = 8: conic plus smooth quartic (k = k ′ = 1) or irreducible 3-nodal
quintic plus line (k = 0, k ′ = 1) or irreducible 8-nodal sextic
viii) 5 ≤ ν ≤ 7: irreducible (ν − 5)-nodal quintic plus line (k = 0, k ′ = 1);
ix) 0 ≤ ν ≤ 7: irreducible ν-nodal sextic.
All these configurations can be realized over the reals, and we get in this way,
as we shall further see, the classification of the nodal Severi varieties of K3 surfaces
of degree 2.
We mention here a result of [Kal86], theorem 5.6, where, following ideas of
Togliatti, he constructed degree six K3 surfaces with 15 nodes (we do not reproduce
the full proof since we reobtain this result as a consequence of Nikulin’s theorems
on quadratic forms).
Proposition 63. There exists a configuration in the plane, of a nodal curve
B of degree six consisting of two smooth conics {Q1 = 0}, {Q2 = 0}, plus 2 lines
{Q3 = 0}, and of two smooth conics C1 = {q1 = 0}, C2 = {q1 = 0}, both not
passing through the nodes of B, and everywhere tangent to B in distinct points,
indeed such that
Q1 Q2 Q3 = r2 + 4q0 q1 q2 ,
where r is a cubic form, and q0 is the square x20 of a linear form.
Let p : Y → P2 be the double cover branched over B, yielding a nodal degree
two K3 surface Y , which is birational to the degree six K3 surface Y ′ ⊂ P4 ,
y0 y1 + q0 (x0 , x1 , x2 ) = 0, y0 q1 (x0 , x1 , x2 ) + y1 q2 (x0 , x1 , x2 ) + r(x0 , x1 , x2 ) = 0.
Y ′ has 15 nodes.
Proof. (Idea) The points e0 , e1 are nodes for Y ′ , and projection from the line
{x0 = x1 = x2 = 0} joining them yields a double cover of the plane branched on

1.7. NODAL K3 SURFACES OF ARBITRARY DEGREE.

57

B: actually projection from e1 yields the nodal quartic surface of equation
y02 q1 (x) − q0 (x)q2 (x) + r(x)y0 = 0,
Since B has 13 nodes, we see that Y ′ has 13 + 2 = 15 nodes.

□

For our application, we need however a stronger result, namely a K3 surface
which is defined over R, and with all the nodes being real points.
This was achieved in [Cat21], where the following theorem was proven:
Theorem 64. For each degree d = 4m there is a nodal K3 surface X ′ of degree
d with 16 nodes which is defined over R, and such that all its singular points are
defined over R.
Similarly, for each degree d = 4m − 2 there is a nodal K3 surface X ′′ of degree
d with 15 nodes which is defined over R, and such that all its singular points are
defined over R.
1.7.1. Codes for K3 surfaces of degree d ≡ 6 (mod 8). Next, we shall
now classify all the pairs of codes as in proposition 60 with m odd; later we shall
state analogues of the theorem concerning nodal quartic surfaces in P3 .
The following theorem is due to collaboration with Michael Kiermaier.
Theorem 65. Let S be a set of cardinality ν ≤ 15, and consider a non-zero
bicoloured code K′ ⊂ FS2 ⊕ F2 H, such that
(i) the code K := K′ ∩ FS2 admits only the weight 8,
(ii) the weights of vectors in K′ are divisible by 4,
(iii) dim(K) ≥ ν − 11, dim(K′ ) ≥ ν − 10, dim(K′ ) ≤ dim(K) + 1.
Then K is a shortening of the simplex code, in particular its dimension k ≤
4, and we can view S ⊂ P := P(F42 ), and the non-zero elements of K as the
characteristic functions of complements of hyperplanes H ⊂ P with the property
that T := (P \ S) ⊂ H.
We have several cases :
(1) K = K′ : k = k ′ = 1, 2, accordingly ν ∈ {8, 9, 10, 11}, resp. ν = 12;
(2) K ̸= K′ , the weight 16 occurs in K′ : then ν = 15 and K′ is the strict
Kummer code;
(3) K =
̸ K′ , all the weights are 8: then k ′ = 1, 2, 3, 4, ν ∈ {7, 8, 9, 10, 11},
resp.ν = 11, 12, resp. ν = 13, resp. ν = 14.
(4) K ̸= K′ , there exists a weight 4 vector (if k > 0 this condition is implied
by the condition that there exists a weight 12 vector): then k = 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, and
respectively ν ∈ {4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, 11}, resp. ν ∈ {9, 10, 11, 12}, resp. ν ∈ {12, 13},
resp. ν = 14, resp. ν = 15. The bicoloured code K′ in this case (4) is unique for
all pairs k, ν except for k = 1, ν ≥ 11, when we have two cases, and also for
k = 2, ν = 13, where we have two cases.
(5) K ̸= K′ , k ′ = 1, ν = 11, there is only the weight 12 in K′ .

58

1. GENERALITIES AND NODAL K3 SURFACES

Proof. In the first case, as we already saw, K = K′ is determined by its dimension k = k ′ ∈ {1, 2, 3, 4}, and then its length n equals, respectively, 8, 12, 14, 15.
Since we have n ≤ ν ≤ 10 + k, only the cases k = 1, 2 are possible.
In the second case obviously ν = 15, hence k ′ ≥ 5, so we have the the strict
Kummer code.
In the third case again K ⊂ K′ are determined by their dimensions k = k ′ −
1, k ′ ∈ {1, 2, 3, 4} and again we have that n ≤ ν + 1 ≤ 11 + k ′ , hence all the cases
k ′ = 1, 2, 3, 4 are possible.
The remaining cases are more interesting, with exception of the subcases k = 0,
which are straightforward to discuss: there is only a non zero weight, and this can
be either 4 or 12, but in the latter case we have ν ≤ 11, ν ≥ 11 ⇒ ν = 11, and we
are in case (5).
Assume that k > 0: then let u be a vector with w(u) = 4. We denote as usual
by v1 the vector corresponding to the first two columns (here H corresponds to
the entry in the last row and last column).
Let a be the number of non-zero coordinates of u in the first two columns, and b
the number of non-zero coordinates in the last two columns, and not the coordinate
of H (that is, we are looking at the code K′′ , projection from H of the code K′ );
then a + b = 3, and adding v1 we must have (8 − a) + b = 5 + 2b ≡ 3 ( mod 4),
which means that b = 1, 3. We have two cases: (a, b) = (2, 1) or (a, b) = (0, 3),
distinguished by the non occurrence - occurrence of the weight 12 in K′ .
Conversely, if the weight 12 occurs, and k = 1, then a+b = 11 and 19−2a ≡ 3 (
mod 4), hence a is even. However, by the B-inequality, ν ≤ 12, hence b ≤ 4, hence
b = 3 (and we have the previous case).
For the respective dimensions k = 4, 3, 2 we must have ν = 15, resp. ν = 14,
resp. ν ∈ {12, 13}.
These are the respective cases where we remove from P a set T of cardinality
0, 1, or just two or three points contained in two hyperplanes, hence two or three
collinear points.
K′′ is spanned by the characteristic function of a subset A ⊂ S = P \ T which
has 3 or 11 elements. Moreover, for each hyperplane H containing T , if A contains
b points in H, and a points not in H, then we must have that b+8−a ≡ 3 (mod 4).
For a + b = 3 this means as before that b = 1, 3. For a + b = 11 this means that b
is odd, and since a ≤ (ν − 8) ≤ 7, b = 5, 7 (then respectively a = 6, 4).
Assume that there is a vector of K′ corresponding to such a set A of cardinality
3. Each plane H must contain either all the 3 elements of A, or just one. Said in
other words, every plane containing T intersects A in only one point, or contains
A.
If T has 0 elements, this means that A consists of three collinear points.
If T has 1 element T , let {A1 , A2 , A3 } = A: then two points in A cannot be
collinear with T , else there are planes H containing two point of A. There is a
plane H ′ containing A and T , and this case is always combinatorially equivalent

1.7. NODAL K3 SURFACES OF ARBITRARY DEGREE.

59

to the one where the three points in A are collinear, and the line Λ they span does
not contain T .
If T has 3 elements, which form a line L, there are a priori two possibilities:
projection from L to a skew line Λ maps the three points Ai to three different
points (this case is combinatorially equivalent to the one where the three points
in A are collinear, and the line Λ they span is skew to L), or there exists a plane
H ′ containing L and A (combinatorially, there is only one case).
However, the second case is not possible: since adding to the characteristic
function of A the characteristic function of the complement of a plane H containing
L and different from H ′ we would get the characteristic function of a set B of
cardinality 8 − 3 = 5, which is a contradiction.
Let us look at the case where T consists of two points T1 , T2 ∈ L, and let us
denote by T3 the third point of L.
If T3 ∈ A, then each plane H containing L contains T3 , and if T3 = A3 , there
is a plane containing A1 and A2 , which is combinatorially equivalent to the case
where the points of A are collinear, but L and A are lines incident in T3 = A3 .
If A ⊂ P \ L, again we have either three collinear points, spanning a line A
skew to L, or there is a plane H ′ containing L and A, but this gives a contradiction
as before.
These are exactly three different cases.
Finally, if there is no such set A of cardinality 3, we have that elements of
′′
K \ K correspond to subsets B of cardinality 11. Then each plane H (containing
T ) must contain exactly 5 or 7 points of B.
The second alternative is not possible if T is non-empty, since T ⊂ H, B ∩ T =
∅.
If T is empty, then B consists of a plane H1 and 4 other points. Hence there
are two planes H1 , H2 such that either B = H1 ∪ H2 , or there are points P, P ′ such
that B = ((H1 ∪ H2 ) \ {P }) ∪ {P ′ }.
Take a plane H containing the line H1 ∩ H2 and different from these two
planes: then adding the characteristic function of the complement of H we get the
characteristic function of a set A with either 3 or 4 points: in both cases we reach
a contradiction.
We can therefore assume that B is such that every hyperplane H (containing
T ) must contain exactly 5 points of B. Then T has at most two elements, and if
it has two elements, for each plane H containing T we have
(H \ T ) ⊂ B ⇒ B = P \ T ,
a contradiction.
If T is one point, take two points P, P ′ not in B, and a plane H containing
T , P, P ′ : then H contains less than 4 points of B, a contradiction. Similarly if T
is empty, take a plane H containing three points in the complement of B.
□

Corollary 66. (I) We have two codes as above for ν = 15, two also for
ν = 14, three for ν = 13, five for ν = 12, seven for ν = 11, four for ν = 9, 10,
three for ν = 8, two for ν = 7, and just one for ν ∈ {4, 5, 6}. For a total of 35
nontrivial bicoloured codes K′ .
(II) All the above bicoloured codes are shortenings of those two with ν = 15.
(III) Case (3) with ν = 14 is a shortening of case (4) with ν = 15.
Proof. Assertion (I) is a direct consequence of the previous theorem, assertion
(II) is a consequence of its proof.
Indeed, cases (1) and (3) are clearly shortenings of case (2), while cases (4)
contemplate always the case where A is a line, hence they shortenings of the case
(4) with ν = 15. If we take the shortening of case (4) with ν = 15 corresponding
to a projective basis of P3 , then we get case (5).
Concerning assertion (III), recall that S is here the projective space P3F2 , the
code K′ is generated by the characteristic functions of complements of planes π,
and by the characteristic function χA , where A is a line.
The vectors in K′′ \ K correspond to χA and χA + 1 + χπ , and if π ⊃ A we get
the characteristic function of a set of cardinality 11 containing A. If A ∩ π = {P },
then we get the characteristic function of a set of cardinality 7 containing exactly
the point P of A.
Hence, if we take the shortening corresponding to the complement of a point
of A, we get a code whose weights are just 7, 8.
□
We borrow the two following results from [Kal86], and we provide proofs for
them since the Thesis [Kal86] is not so easily accessible (even if for the first result
we shall give another proof in Theorem 75).
Proposition 67. Let Y be a nodal complete intersection of type (2, 3) in P4
and let N be an half-even set of nodes on S. Then we have
a) its cardinality t := |N | satisfies t ≤ 11,
b) its associated sheaf is Cohen-Macaulay,
c) the associated double cover is a regular surface.
In particular the extended code K′ of Y cannot be the strict Kummer code (case
(2) of the previous classification in Theorem 65).
