2.6. Geometry and deformations of nodal Segre cubic hypersurfaces
Let n = 2k. Recall that the Segre cubic hypersurfaces are defined by
XS : (x1 + · · · + xn = x31 + · · · + x3n = 0) ⊂ Pn−1
and their nodes form the Sn -orbit of (1k : −1k ) where αs means α is repeated s
times.
The Goryunov–Kalker (GK) cubic hypersurfaces are defined by
XGK : (x1 + · · · + xn + 2xn+1 = x31 + · · · + x3n + 2x3n+1 = 0) ⊂ Pn
and their nodes are the Sn -orbit of the point (1k−1 : −1k+1 : 1).
If n = 4, then the Segre hypersurface is a plane cubic curve with three nodes
i.e. a triangle! Whereas, the GK surface is the Cayley cubic surface. So we will
restrict ourselves to the case n = 2k ≥ 6.
In this section we prove Theorem 117 namely we show
i) The Segre cubic 3-fold (n = 6) is unique and is unobstructed.
ii) The Goryunov–Kalker cubic 4-fold (n = 6) has non-trivial equisingular
deformations and is unobstructed.
iii) The Segre cubic 5-fold and the Goryunov–Kalker 6-fold are unobstructed
and locally rigid.
iv) All other Segre cubics and Goryunov–Kalker cubics are locally rigid and
have obstructed deformations.
Let H be the hyperplane (x1 + · · · + xn = 0) ⊂ Pn−1 respectively (x1 + · · · +
xn + 2xn+1 = 0) ⊂ Pn . The short exact sequence
(13)

0 → J (3) → OH (3) → OH /J =: T → 0

induces the evaluation map
ev : H 0 (H, OH (3)) → H 0 (T ).
Here J is the Jacobian ideal of X ⊂ H and T is supported onLthe singular
locus of X. Indeed, since X has ordinary double points, H 0 (T ) = P ∈Sing X CP .
According to Burns–Wahl ([BW74]) the deformations are unobstructed if the
evaluation map is surjective, i.e. the nodes impose linearly independent conditions
on the space of cubic hypersurfaces.
We say that X is locally rigid if any equisingular deformation of X is projectively equivalent to X itself. This condition is fulfilled if the kernel of the
evaluation map is spanned by the image of X under the action of the space of infinitesimal automorphisms. This occurs in every case except the GK-cubic 4-fold,
where H 0 (J (3)) contains an extra 5-dimensional space of cubics.

2.6. GEOMETRY AND DEFORMATIONS OF NODAL SEGRE CUBICS

117

The evaluation map is Sn -equivariant and we prove the theorem by decomposing the various vector spaces into irreducible Sn -representations and applying
Schur’s lemma.
The proof is organized as follows. In Section 2.6.1 we recall some well-known
facts about the irreducible representations of the symmetric group Sn . In Section 2.6.2 and 2.6.3 we describe the evaluation map of the Segre cubics and the
Goryunov–Kalker cubics, and we prove the theorem. In Section 2.6.4 we give the
decomposition of H 0 (T ) into irreducible representations.
2.6.1. Representation theory of the symmetric group. We briefly recall
some background on Young tableaux, tabloids and Specht modules. We refer to
James’ book [Jam78] for proofs and further details.
There is a correspondence between representations of the symmetric group Sn
and partitions λ := (λ1 , . . . , λm ) of n, where λ1 ≥ · · · ≥ λm . To a partition, one
associates a Young diagram, consisting of m rows each containing λi boxes. A
Young tableau is a Young diagram with shape λ where the boxes are labelled by
1, . . . , n. A Young tableau is called standard if it has strictly increasing rows and
columns.
Definition 131. A Young tabloid of shape λ is an equivalence class of Young
tableaux of shape λ, with respect to the relation where two tableaux are equivalent
if they differ by a permutation of the labelling of the rows.
The symmetric group Sn acts on the set of Young tableaux of shape λ by
permuting the labelling. The column stabiliser CT of a Young tableau T is the
subgroup of Sn which permutes the labelling of T leaving the columns invariant.
1 5 6

Fix a partition λ and consider the C-vector space W generated by Young
tabloids of shape λ. That is, the elements are formal C-linear combinations of
Young tabloids.
Definition 133. For a fixed Young tableau T , we define the Specht vector eT
by
eT =

X

σ∈CT

where [X] denotes the tabloid corresponding to the tableau X.
The Specht module Sλ is the linear subspace of W spanned by the Sn -orbit
of eT where T has shape λ. This is a cyclic Sn -module generated by all Specht
vectors of shape λ.

Theorem 135. [Jam78, §§4–7] In characteristic 0, the Specht modules Sλ
give all the irreducible representations of Sn . A basis of Sλ is given by the Specht
vectors corresponding to standard tableaux of shape λ.

symmetric group Sn acts in a natural way on the subspace

representation called the standard representation V(n−1,1) of Sn . The partition
n = (n − k) + 1 + · · · + 1 corresponds to the k-th exterior product of the standard
representation (cf. [FH91, §4.1]).

Lemma 139. Let λ be a partition of n as in Proposition 137, let T be a Young
tableau of shape λ, and let eT be the corresponding generator.
i) If τ ∈ CT ⊂ Sn preserves the columns of T , then τ (eT ) = eτ (T ) =
sign(τ )eT .
ii) If γ in SC ⊂ Sn permutes only the C-coordinates then γ(eT ) = eγ(T ) = eT .
iii) For any permutation ρ in Sn , we have ρ(eT ) = eρ(T ) .

2

iv) The set of all eT ’s, with T standard gives a basis of the representation Vλ .
Proof. We give an elementary proof for the third row λ = (n−2, 2) of Table 1.
The proof in the other cases works in exactly the same way, but to write everything
out is repetitive. Here we consider Young tableaux T of shape (n − 2, 2):
T =

a1 a2 c1 . . . cn−4
b1 b2

i) if τ preserves the columns then τ (eT ) = (xτ (a1 ) − xτ (b1 ) )(xτ (a2 ) − xτ (b2 ) )σ1 (xC ) =
eτ (T ) = sign(τ )eT .
ii–iii) Using i), it suffices to prove the statements for transpositions of the first
row of T . There are three cases. If ρ is a permutation of the C-coordinates, then
clearly ρ(eT ) = eρ(T ) because σ1 (xC ) is invariant under permutations of C. If ρ
swaps say a1 with c1 , then ρ(eT ) = (xc1 − xb1 )(xa2 − xb2 )σ1 (xρ(C) ) = eρ(T ) . If ρ
swaps two elements of A, then ρ(eT ) = (xa2 − xb1 )(xa1 − xb2 )σ1 (xC ) = eρ(T ) .
iv) Suppose now T is standard. Then the last two monomials of eT in lexicographic
order are xb1 xb2 xcn−3 + xb1 xb2 xcn−4 . Since the entries of T increase along rows and
columns, these trailing terms are pairwise distinct. Thus the standard eT are
linearly independent.
There is a general algorithm applying the so-called Garnir relations recursively
to write any nonstandard generator eT as a linear combination of standard generators (cf. [Jam78, §7]). In our case the algorithm terminates particularly quickly
because T has only two rows.
Step 1: Suppose eT is a nonstandard generator. Using i) we may assume the
columns are ordered and using ii) we may assume that the C-part is ordered.
Step 2: If a2 > c1 then we use the Garnir relation eT −(a2 , c1 )eT +(a2 , c1 , b2 )eT = 0
and possibly column permutations to write eT as a linear combination of two
generators eT1 and eT2 with a2 (Ti ) < c1 (Ti ) for i = 1, 2. We then apply Step 3
to each of these.
Step 3: If a1 > a2 then we use the Garnir relation eT −(a1 , a2 )eT +(a1 , a2 , b1 )eT = 0
and possibly column/C-part permutations to write eT as a linear combination
of generators having a1 < a2 .
We then restart from Step 1 with each of the new generators.
We prove directly that the Garnir relations hold for the third row of Table 1.
For step 2, we first write C ′ := C ∖ {c1 } and observe that
(xa2 − xb2 )σ1 (xC ) − (xc1 − xb2 )σ1 (xC ′ ∪{a2 } ) + (xc1 − xa2 )σ1 (xC ′ ∪{b2 } ) =
= ((xa2 − xb2 ) − (xc1 − xb2 ) + (xc1 − xa2 )) σ1 (xC ′ )
+ (xa2 − xb2 )xc1 − (xc1 − xb2 )xa2 + (xc1 − xa2 )xb2 = 0 + 0

2.6.2.2. The evaluation

We denote the basis vector 1P in CP by a 2 × k labelled array
p1 . . . pk ← positive
pk+1 . . . pn ← negative
where {p1 , . . . , pn } = {1, . . . , n}. The first row corresponds to the coordinates
equal to 1, and the second row to coordinates equal to −1. This implicitly involves
a choice of positive and negative row of the array. Swapping the rows multiplies by
−1 in CP . Clearly, any permutation of the xi which preserves the rows corresponds
to the same point in Pn−1 , and so does any permutation which swaps the two rows
(after applying the C∗ -action on Pn−1 ). For example, for n = 6 we have

Proof. Clearly it is enough to prove the statement for a generator eT in

Proposition 142. The evaluation map ev : Sym3 V → H 0 (T ) has kernel isomorphic to V(n) ⊕ V(n−1,1) ⊕ V(n−2,2) ⊕ V(n−2,1,1) and image isomorphic to V(n−1,1) ⊕
V(n−3,3) .
Proof. By Schur’s Lemma and Lemma 141 it suffices to consider the evaluation map on generators of each irreducible representation. It is easy to see that the
evaluation map is identically zero on V(n) ⊕ V(n−2,1,1) by considering the generators
listed in Table 1.
Fix now a generator fT of V(n−2,2) . For each i = 1, 2 we may assume that the ai and bi -coordinates of P have opposite sign, otherwise xai − xbi would vanish. Thus
exactly one half of the remaining c1 , . . . , cn−4 -coordinates of P are positive, and
one half negative. Hence σ1 (xC ) vanishes at P which implies eT (P ) also vanishes.
Clearly, a generator fT of shape (n − 3, 3) is nonzero on P with ai - and bi coordinates of opposite sign, for i = 1, 2, 3. Note that n ≥ 6 and so fT is not in
the kernel.
Both copies of V(n−1,1) in Sym3 V map isomorphically to the same subspace
of H 0 (T ). Indeed, let P be any point whose a- and b-coordinate have opposite
sign, and let fT , gT generators for the symmetric and the antisymmetric summand,
then fT (P ) and gT (P ) are both nonzero. On the other hand σ2 (xC ) = 1 − k: the
coefficient of z 2k−2 in the polynomial (z 2 − 1)k−1 . Whence
(nfT + (1 − k)gT )(P ) = ±2[nσ2 (xC ) + (1 − k)(−2 − s2 (xC ))]
= ±2[n(1 − k) + (1 − k)(−2 − (n − 2))] = 0
Thus the subspace generated by nfT + (1 − k)gT for T of shape (n − 1, 1) is in the
kernel. That is a copy of the irreducible representation V(n−1,1) .
□

2.6.2.3. Representation of Endomorphisms. If X : (f (x) = 0) is a hypersurface
in H ∼
= P(V ) then any linear endomorphism φ : V → V induces an infinitesimal
2
deformation X̃ of X defined
P by (f (x + εφ(x)) = 0) in H × Spec C[ε]/(ε ). Since
f (x + εφ(x)) = f (x) + ε i φi (x)fxi , where fxi are the partial derivatives of f , we
see that the generic fibre of X̃ also contains the singular locus of X. Thus we have
a map from End V to the kernel of the evaluation map. Recall that X is locally
rigid if the kernel of the evaluation map is equal to the image of End V .
Lemma 144. The Sn -representation End V decomposes as

Theorem 145. The Segre cubic XS ⊂ Pn−1 is locally rigid for all n ≥ 6.
Proof. By Schur’s Lemma, Proposition 142 and Lemma 144, we have an
isomorphism between the image of End V and the kernel of the evaluation map
for all n ≥ 6, whence X is locally rigid.
□

Proposition 150. Let n ≥ 8. The evaluation map ev : Sym3 V → H 0 (T ) has
⊕2

Proof. Using the determinantal description of a generator eT of V(n−2,1,1)
given in Remark 138 we see immediately that eT (P ) vanishes for any singular
point P and thus eT is in the kernel.
A generator fT of shape (n − 3, 3) is nonzero on a point P with ai - and bi coordinates of opposite sign, for i = 1, 2, 3. Clearly, such a singular point exists
on X if and only if k − 1 ≥ 3, i.e. n ≥ 8. If n = 6 then V(n−3,3) is contained in the
kernel of the evaluation map.

2.6. GEOMETRY AND DEFORMATIONS OF NODAL SEGRE CUBICS

125

Both copies of V(n−2,2) in Sym3 V map isomorphically to the same subspace of
H 0 (T ). For a given tableau T consider the generators fT and hT . For each i = 1, 2
we may assume that the ai - and bi -coordinates of P have opposite sign, otherwise
xai −xbi would vanish. Thus exactly k −3 of the remaining c1 , . . . , cn−4 -coordinates
of P are positive, and k − 1 are negative. Hence σ1 (xC ) = −2 and xn+1 = 1, which
implies that fT and hT do not vanish at P , but fT + 2hT does.
The remaining two cases are similar:
Two copies of V(n) are in the kernel and one copy is in the image, because lT
evaluates to 1 but fT +2(k −1)lT and hT +(k −2)lT evaluate to zero for any singular
point P . Three copies of V(n−1,1) are in the kernel and one copy is in the image,
because fT + (k − 4)kT , gT + kkT , hT + 2kT evaluate to 0 for any P , although kT
evaluates to ±2 for certain P .
□

saturation of the Jacobian ideal J requires five cubic generators. In general, the
extra generators of the saturation have degree n − 3.
We have seen in Corollary 59 that the projection from an ordinary double point
P on a cubic 4-fold X has an associated K3 surface S of degree 6, and vice versa.
The K3 surface is the intersection of the cubic with the projectivised tangent cone at
P . Alternatively, S ⊂ P4 is defined by equations A2 (x1 , . . . , x5 ) = B3 (x1 , . . . , x5 ) =

0 where the Taylor expansion of X at the node Px6 := (0, 0, 0, 0, 0, 1) is Ax6 + B. If
X has 15 ordinary double points, then S has 14 A1 singularities. Such K3 surfaces
with 14 A1 singularities have 19 − 14 = 5 moduli, which should account for the
extra summand in the kernel.

Lemma 155. Let T be a Young tableau of shape λ = (n − p, p) with p odd. The
vT satisfy the following properties:
i) Let τ ∈ CT , then τ (vT ) = vτ (T ) = sign(τ )vT .
ii) If γ ∈ SC permutes the C-coordinates, then γ(vT ) = vγ(T ) = vT .
iii) For any ρ ∈ Sn , we have ρ(vT ) = vρ(T ) .
iv) The set of vT for all standard tableaux T forms a basis of the representation
Vλ .
Proof. The proof is rather similar to that for polynomials (cf. Lemma 139).
Thus we only prove ii), because there the distinguished affine chart is important.
ii) Suppose γ is a transposition of elements in the C-part. Let T ′ := γ(T ).
Since T and T ′ differ only in their C-parts, we have CT = CT ′ . Also, XC = XC ′ so
the only difficulty is the distinguished affine chart.
If γ does not involve the last entry c2l , then the affine chart is the same and since
the column stabilisers and set of nodes are equal, we see that γ(vT ) = vT = vγ(T ) .
+
Now consider the transposition γ = (cx , c2l ). Let P = PP − be a node of XC .
If cx is in P + then P = γ(P ) and so P lies in both distinguished affine charts,
hence any summands of vT involving P also appear in vT ′ and γ(vT ). Now suppose
−
cx is in P − . Then PP + lies in the cx distinguished affine chart of XC ′ . Thus any
summand of vT involving P uniquely determines a complementary summand of
vT ′ appearing with opposite sign:

τ (B) ∪ P −

The coefficient is −1 because we have applied the order two permutation pi=1 (ai , bi )
in CT ′ which has sign −1 because p is odd. The two sides of the above displayed
formula are then equal as elements in H 0 (T ), using the C∗ -action on Pn−1 . Hence
vT = vγ(T ) and an analogous argument shows that γ(vT ) = vT .
□

PART 3
Nodal Quintics
Fabrizio Catanese, Alessandro Verra

3.1. Codes of nodal quintic surfaces
In this section we shall describe the codes of nodal quintics, in particular we
shall give new proofs of the code theoretical part of Beauville’s theorem µ(5) = 31.
Recall that ([Bea80], see also section 3.7 of [CC97]) on quintic surfaces even
sets of nodes have cardinality 16 or 20 (these are the weights of K), and they
correspond to representations of the surface as the determinant of a symmetric
matrix with (homogeneous) polynomial entries.
If all the weights are 16, then K is a shortening of the biduality code (code of
a Togliatti quintic with 31 nodes), and these codes all occur because there is an
unobstructed quintic with 31 nodes (see theorems 38 and 166).
The major interest is then to investigate the codes where the weight 20 occurs.
This is the first basic result in this direction.
Theorem 161. Assume that Y is a nodal quintic surface, and that N is an
even set of nodes of cardinality 20.
Then the number ν of nodes of Y is at most 28.
We refer the reader to the list of Appendix B for a classification of all the codes
with weights 16 or 20, with a sublist of those satisfying the B-inequality.
From this list it appears that not all the above codes which satisfy the Binequality do indeed occur, since we shall show that if the weight 20 occurs, then
ν ≤ 28.
With the inequality ν ≤ 28 there are only two interesting codes where the
weight 20 occurs, namely we have:
Lemma 162. Assume that K ⊂ Fν2 has only weights 16 or 20, that the weight
20 occurs and that ν ≤ 28.
Then k := dim(K) ≤ 2, and if k = 2 there are two unique codes, one with
weights (20, 20, 16), the other with weights (20, 16, 16). In the first case the length
n = 28, in the second the length n = 26.
Proof. Let v ∈ K ⊂ FS2 be a vector with weight 20, let A := supp(v),
B := S \ A.
130

3.1. CODES OF NODAL QUINTIC SURFACES

131

If there is another vector u different from v, 0, then set
a := | supp(u) ∩ A|, b := | supp(u) ∩ B|.
Then b ≤ 8 and if a + b = 20, then 20 − a + b = 16, 20 ⇒ a = b + 4, b.
The second case is excluded since then a = b = 10, a contradiction. In the first
case a = 12, b = 8.
If instead a + b = 16, and 20 − a + b = 16, then a = 10, b = 6.
To finish the proof it suffices to show that k < 3, i.e., that k = 3 is not possible.
Assume that there are two vectors v, u of respective weights 20, 20, hence a =
12, b = 8, or of respective weights 20, 16, hence a = 0, b = 6.
Assume that there is another vector v ′ ∈ K, linearly independent of v, u.
Denote by A1 := A ∩ supp(u), |A1 | = 12, 10, set A2 := A \ A1 , so |A2 | = 8, 10.
Then we define a1 , a2 , b′ through the cardinalities of the respective intersections
of supp(v ′ ) with A1 , A2 , B. In particular, b′ = 6 or 8, correspondingly a1 + a2 = 10
or 12.
Since also the following numbers belong to the set {16, 20}:
20 − a1 + a2 − b′ , 16 + a1 − a2 − b′ ,
and they equal in the first case
14 − 2a1 + 10 = 24 − 2a1 , or 2a1 ⇒ 24 = 24 − 2a1 + 2a1 ≥ 32,
a contradiction. In the second case we get
12 − 2a1 + 12 = 24 − 2a1 , or 2a1 − 4 ⇒ 20 = 24 − 2a1 + 2a1 − 4 ≥ 32,
again a contradiction.

□

Proof. (of theorem 161). There is a symmetrix matrix of linear forms
A(x) = (ai,j (x)), i, j = 1, . . . 5,
such that Y is equal to {(x)|det(A(x)) = 0}, while
N = {(x)| corank(A(x)) = 2}.
Y is the projection in P3 of a complete intersection Ŷ inside P3 × P4 of 5
hypersurfaces of bidegree (1, 1).
Ŷ := {(x, y)|

5

ai,j (x)yj = 0}.

In particular, KŶ ∼
= OŶ (1, 0) and p : Ŷ → Y is a partial resolution of Y , of the
nodes in N .
In fact, at the points x where corank(A(x)) = 2 the fibre of p is a P1 , and it is
easy to verify that Ŷ is smooth at the points of this exceptional curve.
At the other points P of Y , where corank(A(P )) = 1, the matrix is equivalent
(as a quadratic form with coefficients in the local ring OP3 ,P ) to a diagonal matrix
Diag(1, 1, 1, 1, f (x)) where then f (x) = 0 is the local equation of Y at P .

Then the equations of Ŷ in the fibre over P are:
y1 = y2 = y3 = y4 = 0, f (x) = 0,
hence we have a local isomorphism Ŷ ∼
= Y.

so that we can eliminate y1 , y2 , y3 , y4 and we have a singular point if and only if
A(1)5,5 = A(2)5,5 = A(3)5,5 = 0.
The above conditions amount to requiring that e′5 is a base point for the system
of quadrics.
Therefore (e0 , e′5 ) is a singular point of Ŷ if and only if e′5 is a base point for the
system of quadrics, and a singular point for the quadric corresponding to A(e0 ),
which has rank 4.
This implies that e′5 is a base point for the 4 quadrics, of multiplicity at least 2.
Conversely, if e′5 is a base point for the 4 quadrics, of multiplicity at least 2, then
the four gradients are not linearly independent, hence there is a quadric which is
singular at e′5 .
Let B be the base locus of the web of quadrics (P3 of quadrics, spanned by the
4 quadrics A(ei )).

3.1. CODES OF NODAL QUINTIC SURFACES

133

If there were a component Γ in B of dimension at least 1, then at the general
point e′ ∈ Γ the gradients would span a space of dimension 3, hence there would
be a singular quadric containing e′ and Ŷ (hence also Y ) would have infinitely
many singular points, a contradiction.
Therefore B is finite and, by Bezout’s theorem, it consists of 16 points counted
with multiplicity. Therefore there can be at most 8 points of multiplicity ≥ 2.
The conclusion is that Ŷ has at most 8 singular points, hence ν(Y ) ≤ 20 + 8 =
28.
□
Remark 163. The same proof works for sextics having a half-even set of cardinality 35, corresponding to a symmetric matrix of linear forms: then ν ≤ 35+16 =
51.
Corollary 164. If the number of nodes of a quintic is at least 29, then the
codewords in K have all weight 16.
In particular Beauville’s theorem: µ(5) ≤ 31, with equality if and only if K is
the simplex (biduality code), follows immediately.
Proof. The first assertion was already shown.
The second follows from the first two McWilliams’ identities, and the Binequality.
As we mentioned already, the McWilliams’ identity reads out as a series of
identities, the first two are:
X
X
ai = 2k − 1,
iai = 2k−1 n.
i>0

i>0

If we have a code K where all the weights are just 2m , let k = dim(K), n its
effective length.
In this case we get
2m (2k − 1) = 2k−1 n ⇒ n = (2k − 1)2(m−k+1) ⇒ k ≤ m + 1.
Here m = 4, moreover, by the B-inequality k ≥ ν − 26, hence ν ≤ 31, equality if
and only if k = 5, n = 31, and the code is seen to be the simplex code. Because
an easy counting argument shows that a basis of K yields a bijection of the nodal
set S with F52 \ {0}.
□
The next question is whether the two codes with k = 2 of lemma 162 are
realized.
For the code with length 28, the answer is positive by virtue of the following
theorem, whose proof shall be given in the following section two (see especially
corollary 171).
Theorem 165. The subset of the Severi variety F(5, 28) such that K admits
the weight 20 is an irreducible component, and the length of K equals 28.
We have then the following main result for the codes of nodal quintics:

134

3. NODAL QUINTICS

Theorem 166. The codes of nodal quintic surfaces are all the shortenings of
the biduality code (simplex) of dimension 5, all the shortenings of the code K of
dimension 2, effective length 28, and weights (20, 20, 16), and possibly also the code
K of dimension 2, effective length 26, and weights (20, 16, 16).
Proof. That all the shortenings occur follows from the unobstructedness of
the Goryunov cubic, by theorem 166, and by the fact that a general symmetroid
(determinant of a 5 × 5 matrix of linear forms) has 20 nodes forming an even set.
We conclude then by lemma 162.
□
Remark 167. It is not yet clear to us whether the code K of dimension 2 and
weights (20, 16, 16) does actually occur.
Remark 168. A more refined and very interesting question is for instance the
one of determining the fundamental groups π1 (Y ∗ ) of nodal maximizing surfaces
of degree d (ν(Y ) = µ(d)). We saw the answer for d ≤ 4, but for d = 5, 6 this is
an open problem.
The case d = 5 appears rather difficult, at least for d = 6 there is a strategy,
by viewing the Barth sextic as a Galois covering of the plane with group (Z/2)3 .
3.2. Prym varieties and nodal quintics with 28 nodes, and code
admitting the weight 20
The following construction is based on the results of [Ver01]:
Theorem 169. Let C be a general canonical curve of genus 5, the complete
intersection of three quadrics Q0 , Q1 , Q2 in P4 ,
C = {Q0 = Q1 = Q2 = 0},
and let η ∈ Pic(C)2 \ {0} be a non trivial 2-torsion divisor class.
Let D be a general divisor in the linear series KC + η.
Let Q3 be a quadric in P4 such that divC (Q3 ) = 2D on C.
Then, if we let Y be the discriminant of the web of quadrics in P4 generated by
Q0 , Q1 , Q2 , Q3 , Y is a nodal quintic surface with 28 nodes and such that the length
of the code K(Y ) (which admits the weight 20) equals 28.
Proof. The linear system |KC + η| is base point free, since if P were a base
point, we would have that h1 (OC (KC +η −P )) ̸= 0, hence the linear system |P +η|
contains an effective divisor, and there is a point P ′ such that 2P ≡ 2P ′ ; since
P ̸= P ′ , the curve C is hyperelliptic, contradicting that it is canonically embedded
in P5 .
Claim 1 : the linear system |KC + η| is birational onto its image for a general
choice of the curve C.
Proof of the claim: otherwise the morphism ϕ associated to |KC +η|, ϕ : C → P3
has image of degree strictly smaller than 8, and greater or equal to 3, hence its

3.2. PRYM VARIETIES AND NODAL QUINTICS WITH 28 NODES

135

image is a curve Γ of degree 4; and ϕ factors through a double covering p : C → C ′ ,
where C ′ cannot have genus 0 since C is not hyperelliptic.
Hence C ′ has genus 1, and is isomorphic to Γ which is a complete intersection
of two quadrics. Since the covering is branched in 8 points, such curves C depend
on 8 moduli, while curves of genus 5 have 12 moduli.
Consequence of claim 1: the monodromy group of the hyperplane sections
of C (the divisors D) is a transitive subgroup.
Choose now a general divisor D ∈ |KC + η|, and let Y be the discriminant of
the web of quadrics in P4 generated by Q0 , Q1 , Q2 , Q3 , where divC (Q3 ) = 2D. In
particular, D consists of 8 distinct points P1 , . . . , P8 and all the quadrics in the
web intersect C in the divisor 2D. And we have
Claim 2 : for each of the points Pi , there is a unique quadric Q(i) in the web
such that Pi ∈ Ker(Q(i)).
Proof of claim 2: there are local coordinates x, y, z, t at P such that Q0 =
x, Q1 = y, Q2 = z, Q3 = t2 + ax + by + cz + g, where a, b, c, ∈ C and g is in the
ideal (x, y, z) and vanishes of order at least 2 at P .
Hence the desired quadric is Q3 − aQ0 − bQ1 − cQ2 .
We observe now that the 8 quadrics Q(i) are all distinct, provided that the
following claim holds:
Claim 3 : each of the quadrics Q(i) has rank at least 4, for D general.
To prove claim 3, by the consequence of claim 1, it suffices to show that these
quadrics cannot all have rank ≥ 3.
Claim 4 : for D general, there are exactly 20 distinct quadrics of rank 3 in
the web.
Observe that claims 3 and 4, by virtue of the argument given in the proof
of theorem 161, show the desired assertion: that Y possesses 20 nodes where
corank(A(x)) = 2, plus other 8 nodes, corresponding to the base points of the web
through which passes a quadric which is singular at the point.
We begin now the main argument by observing first that the locus Σ of rank
≤ 3 quadrics in the web is finite: because the net generated by Q0 , Q1 , Q2 contains
no such quadric, for general choice of the three quadrics (the quadrics of corank
≥ 2 form a subvariety of codimension 3 in the space of quadrics in P4 ).
We consider now the variety
S := {(D, Q)|D ∈ |KC + η|, divC (Q) = 2D, rank(Q) ≤ 3}.
S fibres onto the linear system ∈ |KC + η| with finite fibres Σ, and we want
to show that S is reduced, so that the general fibre consists of 20 distinct points,
thus proving claim 4.
Indeed, from the work of [Ver01], it follows that S splits into two irreducible
components, which map with respective degrees 8, 12.

136

3. NODAL QUINTICS

Then we shall prove claim 3 simply by showing that the component mapping of
degree 8 does not consist of pairs where the quadric Q has vertex ker(Q) containing
a point of C.
A first remark is that, for general D, no quadric Q with divC (Q) = 2D can
have rank equal to 2. Because otherwise there are two hyperplanes H1 , H2 such
that 2D = divC (Q) = divC (H1 ) + divC (H2 ).
Writing divC (Hi ) = 2Di′ + B, we obtain, assuming that for each D there
were such a rank 2 quadric, by monodromy invariance of such decomposition, and
by transitivity of the monodromy group, that B = 0; then D = D1′ + D2′ , and
D1′ , D2′ would be effective thetacharacteristics. Since C is not hyperelliptic, by
Clifford’s theorem, follows that h0 (C, O(Di′ )) ≤ 2, in particular the general divisor
D ∈ |K + η| ∼
= P3 cannot be written as the sum of two such effective divisors
′
′
D1 , D2 .
We can therefore restrict ourselves to the consideration of rank 3 quadrics.
Prym interpretation of rank 3 quadrics.
Assume now that Q is a rank 3 quadric, such that Q · C = 2D, D ∈ |KC + η|
(so in particular, C is not contained in Q).
Projection from the vertex V := ker(Q) is a rational map to P2 with image a
conic q of equation x1 x2 − x23 = 0, so that V = {x1 = x2 = x3 = 0}: we recall that
the rational map is given by setting λ := xx31 = xx32 ∈ P1 . We also denote by Hλ the
tangent hyperplane to Q corresponding to the point λ of the conic q.
We consider now the double cover C̄ → C defined as follows:
C̄ ⊂ C × P1 , C̄ := {(x, λ)|x ∈ Hλ }.
For each point x ∈ C \ Q, x 7→ (x1 , x2 , x3 ) ∈ P2 \ q, and we have two distinct
points y1 , y2 of C̄ lying over x.
Hence C̄ → C is certainly unramified outside Q. If instead x ∈ Q, observe that
C̄ = C ×P2 Z, Z := {(x1 , x2 , x3 , z) ∈ P3 |z 2 = −(x1 x2 − x23 )}.
Since divC (Q) = 2D, the normalization p : C̃ → C̄ is an étale covering of C,
corresponding to η ∈ Pic(C)2 \ {0}: because H ≡ KC , D ≡ KC + η. And C̃ is
connected since η is nontrivial.
Since Z ∼
= P1 × P1 we get two maps ψ+ , ψ− : C̃ → P1 , exchanged by the
covering involution ι : z 7→ −z, since −z 2 + x23 = x1 x2 yields

Recall the definition of the Prym variety (see [Mum74], [Ver01]):
Definition 170. Let p : C̃ → C̄ be an étale covering of C, corresponding to
η ∈ Pic(C)2 \{0} (hence such that, if g is the genus of C, then C̃ has genus 2g −1).
In this situation {L̃|N orm(L̃) = KC } ⊂ P ic2g−2 (C̃) splits into two connected
components, distinguished by the parity of h0 (L̃) = h1 (L̃) = h0 (KC̃ −L̃) = h0 (ι∗ L̃).
P (C, η) := P rym(C̃, C) is defined then as the connected component where the
parity is even, and it is isomorphic to the Abelian subvariety of P ic0 (C̃) which is
the image of 1 − ι∗ .
And the Prym-theta divisor Ξ of P (C, η) consists of the effective divisors inside
P (C, η).
Conversely, giving two pencils inside the respective linear systems |L̃| and ι∗ |L̃|,
exchanged by the covering involution ι, for L̃ such that N orm(L̃) = KC , yields a
subvariety Z ∼
= P1 × P1 inside the linear system p∗ |KC |. It is explained in [Ver01]
p. 228 (2.23) that ι∗ acts on Z, and on P3 with eigenvalues (1, 1, 1, −1), so that
intersecting with the +1 eigenspace we obtain a rank 3 conic q ⊂ P2 , whose points
parametrize divisors of C of the form 2D, where D ∈ |KC + η|.
Now, a point y ∈ C̃ lies in the union of the base loci of the respective pencils
if and only if all divisors coming from Z vanish at y + ι(y), or, equivalently, the
point x := p(y) of the divisor D lies in the vertex of the quadric Q.
Recall now (see [Ver01] theorem 2.5) that we have a finite covering
S → |KC + η|, which has two irreducible components, which we denote by W
and U even , corresponding to the respective cases h0 (L̃) ≡ 1, 0 mod(2).
Here, W parametrizes the quotient of the variety of pairs (L̃, V ) where h0 (L̃) ≥
3 is odd, and V ⊂ H 0 (L̃) s a two-dimensional subspace, by the action of ι. And
similarly for U even .
As a matter of fact, for C general, for any such L̃ ∈ |Ξ| we have h0 (L̃) = 2,
hence it turns out that U even /ι ∼
= Ξ/ ± 1.
We establish now the needed property.
Claim 5 For a general C, for both cases h0 (L̃) ≥ 2 even or odd, there exists a
pair (L̃, V ) where V ⊂ H 0 (L̃) yields a two-dimensional base point free subsystem.
Proof of the claim.
Take a special C such that C = Q1 ∩ Q2 ∩ Q3 , where Q1 , Q2 , Q3 are quadrics
in P4 , Q3 has rank = 3, and Sing(Q3 ) ∩ C = ∅. This is possible, just starting from
C3 and then taking two general quadrics Q1 , Q2 .
Now, the projection with centre the vertex Sing(Q3 ) of Q3 yields a morphism
Ψ : C → P1 corresponding to a thetacharacteristic G on C.

138

3. NODAL QUINTICS

Since C is not hyperelliptic, and the degree of G is 4, it follows by the theorem
of Clifford that h0 (G) ≤ 2, hence equality holds: h0 (G) = 2.
Now, we consider
{G ⊗ η|η ∈ Pic(C)2 \ {0}}.
This set contains even and odd thetacharacteristics, in particular, since as we
have shown h0 (G ⊗ η) ≤ 2, we can choose an η such that h0 (G ⊗ η) = 1, and
also we can choose an η such that h0 (G ⊗ η) = 0 (since Sing(ΘC ) cannot contain
2g−1 (2g + 1) points, see Lemma 4 , pag. 233 of [Igu64], showing that at least one
theta constant is non zero).
We set now L̃ := p∗ (G), where p : C̃ → C is the étale double covering associated
to η.
By the projection formula
H 0 (L̃) = H 0 (G) ⊕ H 0 (G ⊗ η).
So, the dimension h0 (L̃) = 2 + h0 (G ⊗ η) = 3, or 2, and in the second case we
get a base point free pencil, while in the first we get a base point free pencil for
general choice of V , hence for general choice of the divisor D.
By a monodromy argument, we get that for general choice of C and D there
are no quadrics of rank 3 in the web satisfying the property Sing(Q) ∩ C ̸= ∅. □
Corollary 171. In the Nodal Severi variety F(5, 28) the condition that K
contains a codeword of weight 20 determines an irreducible component.
Proof. Y must be the discriminant of a web of quadrics with 8 distinct base
points Pi , for which there is exactly one quadric in the web which is singular at
Pi . Therefore, for general choice of Q0 , Q1 , Q2 in the web, C := Q0 ∩ Q1 ∩ Q2 is
a smooth curve, and divC (Q3 ) = 2D, where D is not contained in a hyperplane,
hence D ≡ KC + η, with η a nontrivial 2-torsion divisor.
Therefore our family is dominated by the family of genus 5 canonical curves,
given together with a Prym-canonical divisor D as above.
□
Appendix B. Codes K with only 16, 20 as weights
Michael Kiermaier

For quintics, the maximum number ν of nodes is 31. This has been shown by
Beauville in [Bea80], where he proved the following important algebro geometric
results:
i) B-inequality: the even sets of nodes of a quintic yield a linear binary [ν, k]
code C with k ≥ ν − 26 , and with
ii) non-zero weights in {16, 20}.
The main purpose of this appendix is to classify the codes satisfying properties
i), ii), respectively only property ii).

APPENDIX B. CODES K WITH ONLY 16, 20 AS WEIGHTS

This is interesting, because it shows, compared with the results of Part III,
that not all of the codes satisying i) and ii) do geometrically occur as codes of a
nodal quintic surface (because of Theorem 161).
In passing we shall also give a third proof of the code theoretic part of this
result (the second proof being the content of Corollary 164) as a consequence of
the MacWilliams identities for the weight enumerator of linear codes, of course
using i) and ii).
We shall call admissible all the codes which satisfy i) and ii), and we shall here
classify them up to isomorphism.

on n. For k ≤ 5, this is weaker than Inequality (14). For k = 6, Inequality (14) is
sharpened to n ∈ {35, 36}. Therefore, the condition k ≥ n − 26 is violated and we
have k ≤ 5, implying that n ≤ k + 26 ≤ 31, which is the result of Beauville.
Let P be the set of points of the projective space PFk−1
. Up to isomorphism,
q
a Fq -linear code C of dimension k and full length n is determined by a multiset
of n points in P, given by the n rows of a generator matrix G (a multiset is a
map C : P → Z≥0 ). Two multisets yield the same code if and only if they are
projectively equivalent (this amounts to a change of basis for C). Moreover, since
C has dimension k, the multiset, that is, the support of the function C, must span
P.
P
For A ⊆ P we consider the multiplicity function C(A) :=
x∈A C(x), and
observe that to a codeword c = Gx corresponds the hyperplane Hx := x⊥ , and
the weight w(c) equals C(P \ Hx ) = n − C(H). Two codewords lead to the same
hyperplane if and only if they yield the same point in P.
By Proposition 16 it follows that the weights of the code vectors determine the
function C(x), hence the code C.
Another way to argue is that the incidence matrix of the points vs. hyperplanes
of is invertible (see [Kun86] section 1.2, page 35), hence, if the multiplicities of
the hyperplanes (or their complements) are fixed, so are the multiplicities C(x) of
the points.
2.3. Classification of the admissible codes. Table 1 shows all remaining
tuples (n, k, a16 , a20 , a⊥
2 ) with k ≥ n − 26. We see that, indeed, there always exists
a code with the given parameters.
All codes are unique up to isomorphism by the following argument. In all cases,
it is obvious from the list that up to isomorphism there is only a single choice to
assign the weights 16 or 20 to the hyperplanes.
Therefore the Radon transform of the multiset C(x) is unique, hence, by Radon
duality, C(x) is unique, hence the code – if one exists – is unique.
So we have:
Theorem 172. If there is a quintic with ν nodes, its even sets of nodes form
a binary linear [ν, k] code with k ≥ ν − 26. After removing the zero coordinates,
the code is isomorphic to one of the codes in Table 1, where each line represents a
single isomorphism type of a linear code.
2.4. Remark on the classification without the dimension restriction.
Without the restriction k ≥ ν − 26, further hypothetical parameters of codes show
up, see Table 2.

classified in [Ton96]. For n = 35 there are 7 isomorphism types and for n = 36
(via the complement with n = 27) there are 5 isomorphism types. The most
symmetric ones have a nice geometric description: for n=36, take the complement

PART 4
Nodal Sextics
Fabrizio Catanese, Yonghwa Cho, Michael Kiermaier

4.1. Half-even sets on nodal sextic surfaces
This section is important for our purposes, even if we cannot yet completely
classify the half-even sets of nodes on nodal sextic surfaces. Indeed, using homological algebra and earlier results, we get some information about the possible
numbers attained by the cardinality t of a half-even set of nodes on a sextic.
It satisfies t ≡ −1 (mod 4) and t ≥ 15. Moreover, it can be 15, 27, 31, 35 but
cannot be 19, 23; using the description of the code of the Barth sextic we see that
also the cases t = 39, 43 occur. We cannot determine in general whether the cases
with t ≥ 47 occur, but we see that this occurrence is not allowed if the number of
nodes ν = 65.
Building from this we achieve here our original aim, to give a sufficient condition
in order that a nodal sextic surface Y be the discriminant of a cubic hypersurface.
The condition is that Y contains a half-even set N of cardinality 31, and such
that a certain linear system, |2H − L|, has dimension zero (observe that, if t = 31,
then necessarily dim|2H − L| ≥ 0: we do not yet know whether the first condition
alone is sufficient).
We set up again the notation: Y is a nodal sextic surface, S := Ỹ is its minimal
resolution, and N is an half-even set of nodes.
We denote by E1 , . . . , Eν the exceptional (−2)-curves, therefore there exists a
divisor L on S such that

contracted to smooth points, yielding p : Z → Z ′ and a finite double covering
f ′ : Z′ → Y .

Proposition 173. The first cohomology groups H 1 (S, OS (mH+L)) and H 1 (S, OS (mH−
L)) are zero for all m if and only if H 1 (S, OS (mH + L)) = 0 for m = 1, 2 or for
m = 0, −1.
Recall that [CC97] on Y we have the so called quadratic sheaf
F := π∗ OS (−L), such that f∗′ (OZ ′ ) = OY ⊕ F.
Corollary 174. h1 (S, OS (mH + L)) = 0 for m = 1, 2 if and only if the
following equivalent conditions hold:
(1) the sheaf F is arithmetically Cohen-Macaulay,
(2) H 1 (F) = H 1 (F(1)) = 0,
(3) N is a symmetric set of nodes, that is, there is a symmetric matrix of
homogeneous polynomials Ai,j (x) such that
Y = {x| det(A(x)) = 0}, N = {x| corank(A(x)) = 2}.
We use now the Riemann-Roch theorem to calculate Euler-Poincaré characteristics.

Lemma 176. The cardinalities of the symmetric sets of nodes depend on the
degrees d1 ≤ d2 ≤ · · · ≤ dp of the diagonal entries of the matrix A(x), which must
be odd numbers.
• degrees (1,5) : t = 15, in this case L is effective (|L| has dimension 0);
• degrees (3,3) : t = 27, in this case L is not effective, but L + H is effective
(|L + H| has dimension 1);
• degrees (1,1,1, 3) : t = 31, in this case L is not effective, but L + H is
effective (|L + H| has dimension 0);
• degrees (1,1,1,1,1,1) : t = 35, in this case L + H is not effective, but
L + 2H is effective.
Proof. We use the inequalities of [Cat81], and of [CC97], page 249, especially: di + dp−1−i > 0.

three numbers 2, 4, 6.

From di ≤ d + δ − 2 follows that di ≤ 5, hence dp ∈ {1, 3, 5}. If d1 ≥ 1, then
all the di ’s are positive and we get the claimed four cases.
Since d1 + dp−2 > 0, follows that d1 ≥ −3, and if d1 = −3, then dp−2 , dp−1 , dp =
5; hence p = 6 and, since the sum yields 6, we must have (−3, −3, −3, 5, 5, 5),
which contradicts however d2 + d3 > 0.
So, the case d1 = −3 is excluded, and similarly the case d1 = −1 is excluded.
Since then dp−2 , dp−1 , dp ≥ 3, hence we must have (−1, −1, −1, 3, 3, 3), which again
contradicts d2 + d3 > 0.
The other assertions follow since L + mH is effective, then also L + mH − ∆
is effective, since (L + mH) · Ei = −1. Hence L + mH is effective if and only if
L + mH − ∆ = (m + 1)H − L is effective, equivalently H 0 (F(m + 1)) ̸= 0.

Remark 177. The cohomology groups of the restriction F|H := F ⊗ OH of
F to a smooth hyperplane section H have respective dimensions (according to the
degrees (1, 5), (3, 3), (13 , 3), (16 )):
• h0 (F|H) = 0,
• h0 (F(1)|H) = 1, 0, 0, 0,
• h0 (F(2)|H) = 3, 2, 1, 0,
• h1 (F|H) = 12,
• h1 (F(1)|H) = 7, 6, 6, 6,
• h1 (F(2)|H) = 3, 2, 1, 0.
It follows that h0 (F) = 0, and h0 (F(1)) = 0 except possibly in the first case.
This case is however clear, since we have
Lemma 178. h0 (F(1)) ̸= 0, equivalently, L is effective, if and only N is symmetric and we are in the first case of lemma 176, and also if and only if t = 15.
Proof. If L is effective, there is a plane H cutting on Ỹ the divisor 2D + ∆,
hence we have a plane z = 0 such that the intersection with Y is twice a curve Γ,
and moreover the plane passes through the nodes of N .

4.1. HALF-EVEN SETS ON NODAL SEXTIC SURFACES

149

Hence the equation F = 0 of Y equals the square of a cubic polynomial
g(x0 , x1 , x2 ), modulo z, hence
F (x0 , x1 , x2 , z) = g(x0 , x1 , x2 )2 + zP (x0 , x1 , x2 , z),
for some degree 5 polynomial P and F is the determinant of the symmetrical
matrix with entries P, g, g, z.
At the nodes of N , g = 0 must be a smooth curve (else the quadratic part of
F would not have rank equal to 3), and the singular points of Y are then clearly
the 15 points where z = P = g = 0. These are the points where the corank of the
symmetrical matrix equals 2.
The last equivalence follows from Theorem 1.10, part (ii) of Endrass, [End98],
showing that t ≥ 15, always, equality holding if and only if h0 (F(1)) ̸= 0.
□
Lemma 179. Assume h0 (F(1)) = 0 and t ≤ 31: then h0 (F(2)) ̸= 0, equivalently, H + L is effective. If h0 (F(2)) = 0, then h1 (OS (H + L)) = 41 (t − 35).
In particular, if h0 (F(2)) = 0 and t = 35, then the set N is symmetric and we
are in the fourth case of Lemma 176.
Proof. We apply the Riemann-Roch inequality to the divisor H + L:
h0 (OS (H+L)) = h0 (OS (H+L))+h0 (OS (H−L)) = h0 (OS (H+L))+h2 (OS (H+L)) ≥

≥ χ(OS (H + L)) = 11 + (−9 − t).

The second assertion follows right away from the previous computation, since
then χ(OS (H + L)) = −h1 (OS (H + L)).
For the third assertion, we know that from h0 (F(2)) = 0 and t = 35 follows
that hi (OS (H + L)) = 0 for all i, equivalently, hi (F(1) = 0 for all i.
The exact cohomology sequence associated to
0 → F(1) → F(2) → F(2)|H → 0
implies then that H 0 (F(1)|H) = 0, whence we are in the last case for the hyperplane section and we also infer that hi (F(2) = 0 for all i.
The exact cohomology sequence associated to
0 → F(2) → F(3) → F(3)|H → 0
shows then that h0 (F(3)) = h0 (F(3)|H) = 6, and that these global sections generate F(3) on the smooth locus, hence everywhere by the reflexivity of F.
We can now just apply theorem 2.23 of [Cat81], or give a direct argument as
follows: we can show that there is an exact sequence
0 → OP3 (−1)6 → OP63 → F(3) → 0
by the analogous result for the restriction to the hyperplane section.

Remark 180. (i) The analysis of the code of the Barth sextic, together with
remark 163 show that there are half-even sets of nodes with t = 35 and not
symmetric, hence with h0 (F(2)) ̸= 0.
It shows moreover that the cases t = 39, t = 43 do indeed occur (t = 39 had
been previously constructed by Barth [Bar80]).
(ii) The same argument as in lemma 179 shows that h0 (F(3)) ̸= 0, equivalently,
2H + L is effective as soon as t < 59.
The cases where t ≥ 47 can be ruled out for ν = 65 by coding theory, see
appendix C.
(iii) Endrass [End98] proved that t = 19, 23 do not occur.
In another vein, we can give an upper bound for t:
Proposition 181. Assume that H + L is effective. Then t ≤ 43.
Proof. If H + L is effective, there is an effective divisor D such that 2D ≡
2H + 2L = 3H + ∆, and since D · ∆ = −1, D = C + ∆, with C effective, hence
2C ≡ 3H − ∆.
Consider the polar system, spanned by the partial derivatives of F : it is a
subsystem of 5H − ∆, and it has no base points, whence (5H − ∆) · C ≥ 0.
We get
0 ≤ (5H − ∆) · (3H − ∆) = 90 − 2t ⇔ t ≤ 45,
and we conclude since t + 1 is divisible by 4.
□

Proposition 183. Assume that t = 31 and that h0 (F(2)) = 1. Then we are
in the symmetric case with degrees (1, 1, 1, 3).
Proof. We have h0 (F(1)) = 0 by lemma 178, so by duality h2 (F(2)) = 0.
Since h0 (F(2)) = h2 (F(1)) = 1 and χ(F(2)) = χ(F(2)) = 1, follows the vanishing
h1 (F(2)) = h1 (F(1)) = 0.
By the exact sequence 0 → F(1) → F(2) → F|H (2) → 0 follows that we are
in the third case for F|H , hence h0 (F|H (1)) = 0.
By the exact cohomology sequence associated to the sequence 0 → F →
F(1) → F|H (1) → 0 follows that h1 (F) ≤ h1 (F(1)) = 0, hence we can apply
corollary 174 and obtain that N is symmetric (the sheaf F is Cohen-Macaulay),
and of the desired form by lemma 176.
□
Remark 184. It is an interesting question to see whether if t = 27 or t = 31
then necessarily the half-even set N is symmetric.
In order to construct symmetric half-even sets of nodes with t = 31 we consider
three distinct half-even sets of nodes N1 , N2 , N3 with t = 15.
We first observe that the sum N1 + N2 of N1 and N2 , that is, the symmetric
difference (N1 ∪ N2 ) \ (N1 ∩ N2 ), is a strictly even set of weight (= cardinality)
t = 24.
In fact, its weight is at most 30, and lies in the set {24, 32, 40, 56} by [CT05].
Hence |N1 ∩ N2 | = 3.
Since half-even sets of nodes N1 , N2 , N3 with t = 15 correspond to nodes lying
in a plane section Hi of Y , the above observation shows that N1 ∩ N2 consists of
three collinear nodes (in the line H1 ∩ H2 ).

152

4. NODAL SEXTICS

Therefore a priori N1 ∩N2 ∩N3 has cardinality 0, 1, 3. Accordingly the half-even
set N1 + N2 + N3 would have cardinality t = 27, 31, 39.
Hence we get t = 31 if and only if the three planes H1 , H2 , H3 are linearly
independent and meet in a node of Y . In this case we shall show that N1 +N2 +N3
is symmetric.
Proposition 185. Assume that we have a nodal sextic Y with three distinct
half-even sets of nodes N1 , N2 , N3 with t = 15, such that N := N1 + N2 + N3 has
cardinality t = 31.

These are 15 points if we have a transversal intersection, which is only possible

node precisely with multiplicity 1.

4.2. Discriminant nodal sextic surfaces
This section is devoted to the study of discriminant sextics, obtained via the
projection to P3 , with centre a plane L ⊂ X, of a cubic hypersurface X of P6 .
4.2.1. Preliminaries on discriminant nodal sextics surfaces. We proceed now to some considerations concerning the case of discriminantal nodal sextics, in view of (4) of Theorem 114.
The finite set Sing(X) ∩ L is the base locus in L ∼
= P2 of the system of quadrics
spanned by Q0 , Q1 , Q2 , Q3 , which can only be either a net or a web, and not a
pencil: otherwise there would be a line {x1 = x2 = 0} where A′ vanishes, hence
this line would be singular for Y .

There is exactly one case with M reducible where Sing(X) ∩ L is empty: M
consists of a quadric cone and a plane intersecting it transversally in a smooth

If the base locus contains three non collinear points, then we may assume b = d = 0,
and since we have a net c(x) is linearly independent from x1 , x2 , so that we may
set c(x) = x3 ; hence we obtain the desired normal form (15), for which the base
locus consists of exactly 3 points, and M consists of 3 general planes.
The base locus cannot contain three collinear points, else it would contain a
line, and be infinite, a contradiction.
If the base locus contains exactly two points, we may assume b = 0, d ̸= 0, and
by rows and columns operations that c(x) = c(x2 , x3 ).

Hence M is the cone over a cubic union of a line and a conic.
If c ≡ 0 we may set d =: x3 and we would get that the base locus consists
of a simple base point and a double one. But this case is excluded since then
det(A) ∈ (x1 , q1 )2 , and Y does not have isolated singularities.
We may assume then that either c(x) = x3 , or d(x) = x3 , c(x) = λx2 , λ ̸= 0.
In the latter case the conic is 2λx22 − x1 x3 = 0, it is smooth, and tangent to the line
x1 = 0. In the former case we get the conic 2x2 x3 −x1 d(x) = 0, intersecting the line
x1 = 0 in two distinct points. The conic is smooth unless d = − 21 λµx1 + λx2 + µx3 ,

156

4. NODAL SEXTICS

in which case with rows and columns operations and with new variables we obtain
the normal form with b = 0, d = λx1 , where λ ̸= 0 (since otherwise we would have
three base points).
For the assertion concerning the hypothesis that M is reducible, we simply run
the list again in the case of a web, and observe that M contains a plane only in
the second case of i), where there are no base points, or in the ‘missing case’ (16)
where M is the union of a plane and a smooth quadric and there are two base
points.
If the net has two base points, then we saw that M is reducible.
We consider next the case of a net where there are no base points, and show
that then M is irreducible.
Otherwise, we can assume that x3 divides the equation of M .
In this case the net yields a morphism ϕ : P2 → P2 of degree 4, and x1 , x2 , x3
are coordinates of the dual plane of the target P2 . The line x3 = 0 is dual to a point
of the target P2 , and if we take a general other point, the pencil of lines through
it pulls back to a general pencil in the net which has 4 distinct base points, hence
it has normal form x1 z1 z2 + x2 z3 (z1 + z2 + z3 ) = 0.
Then the net has the form
2x1 z1 z2 + 2x2 z3 (z1 + z2 + z3 ) + x3 Q3 (z) = 0,
where Q3 (z) does not vanish at the 4 points e1 , e2 , e2 − e3 , e1 − e3 .
The condition that x3 divides the discriminant means that setting x3 = 0, we
get a discriminant which is identically zero as a polynomial in x1 , x2 . But indeed
we get 2x1 x2 (x2 − x1 ) mod(x3 ), a contradiction.

We show now that the case of a net where there is exactly one base point, and
for which M is reducible, does not exist.

Case 0) : there is no rank 1 conic in the net. Hence M is the cone over a
reducible plane curve C, given together with a nontrivial invertible sheaf F of

three general lines, or a line and a smooth conic meeting in two points.
In both cases there is just one normal form since P ic0 (C) = C∗ (see the theorem
of [Bar77] about ineffective theta characteristics, and also [Cat81], Proposition
2.28 and Remark 2.29).

point in the net where the rank of A′ drops down to 1; this case however must be
excluded since then we have three base points of the net.

Theorem 187. Let Y be a nodal sextic surface in P3 with ν(Y ) nodes and a
symmetric half-even set N of cardinality 31: then Y is the discriminant of a nodal
cubic X ⊂ P6 for the projection with centre a plane L ⊂ X.
X has exactly γ(X) = ν(Y ) − 31 − r nodes, where r ∈ {0, 1, 2, 3} is the cardinality of L ∩ Sing(X).
Proof. Since the set N is symmetric, there is a matrix of homogeneous polynomials A(x), where deg Ai,j = 21 (di + dj ), and d1 , d2 , d3 , d4 = 1, 1, 1, 3, and such
that N is the set of points where the matrix A(x) has rank = 2.
Hence Y is the discriminant of a cubic hypersurface, and the assertion follows
directly from Theorem 114 and from theorem 186.
□
In the next subsection we shall show that, in the case ν(Y ) = 65, r can only
be 2, 3, that is, the cubic has then 31 or 32 nodes.
4.2.2. 65 nodal sextics as cubic discriminants. By theorem 227, the (extended) code of Barth’s sextic is isomorphic to the (extended) code of an arbitrary
sextic with 65 nodes.
In particular, any such sextic has 26 half-even sets of cardinality 15. Let
C1 , . . . , C26 be the corresponding reduced plane sections.
Let π : Ỹ → Y be the minimal resolution. For a subset N ⊂ Sing Y of nodes,
we denote by EN the sum of the corresponding (−2)-curves in Ỹ .

sets Nα , Nβ , Nγ of cardinality 15 whose (code-theoretic) sum is N . The proof of
Proposition 185 applies in the same way: let H̃ ∈ |π ∗ OY (1)|, and L ≡ 21 (H̃ + EN ).

Corollary 190. The Barth sextic and every other sextic surface with 65 nodes
is the discriminant of the projection pL of a cubic hypersurface X ⊂ P6 with 31
nodes, with centre a plane L intersecting Sing(X) in three points, and not contained
in a three dimensional linear subspace Λ ⊂ X.
In a nodal sextic with 65 nodes, the number of half-even sets of cardinality 31 is
1690. Amongst them, 1300 admit the decomposition as the sum of three half-even
sets of cardinality 15. Each of those 1300 half-even sets yields a pair (X, L) as in
corollary 190. We observe here that every half-even set of cardinality 31 is either
the sum of three half-even sets of cardinality 15, or the sum of a half-even set of
cardinality 15 with an even set of cardinality 24.
We consider the remaining 390 cases in which N is a 31-half-even set that
admits a Nα + Q decomposition where Nα is an 15-half-even set, and Q is a 24even set cut out by a quadric V ⊂ P3 . Consider the degree 6 curve Q such that
2Q = Y · V , and let Q̃ ⊂ Ỹ be the proper transform of Q. We remark that V is
an irreducible quadric; otherwise, V is a union of two planes, thus Q is a sum of
two 15-half-even sets.
To prove that N is symmetric, we need to study numerical invariants such as
2
Q̃ . To do this, we first regard Q as a divisor in V , and compare intersection
theories between V and Y .

Lemma 191. Let X be a smooth projective variety, and let Q1 , Q2 ⊂ X be
reduced closed curves. Let S, S ′ ⊂ X be smooth projective surfaces containing
Q1 , Q2 . Suppose Q1 and Q2 share no common irreducible components. Then,
(Q1 . Q2 )S = (Q1 . Q2 )S ′ ,
where the subscript indicates the ambient surface where the intersection takes place.
Proof. Let Q = Q1 ∪ Q2 endowed with the reduced structure.
This follows from the exact sequence
0 → OQ → (OQ1 ⊕ OQ2 ) → OQ1 ∩Q2 → 0
defining the intersection multiplicity, so that
(Q1 · Q2 )S = h0 (OQ1 ∩Q2 ) = (Q1 · Q2 )S ′ .
□

Case 1. Assume V is a quadric cone. In this case V ≃ P(1, 1, 2). Let hV ∈

By adjunction formula one may write Q̃21 in terms of e and pa (Q̃1 ). To estimate
the arithmetic genus, we look at the proper transform of Q1 along the resolution
F2 → V .
By [End98, Proposition 2.4], Q1 cuts out at least 4e nodes of Y . If we vary
Q1 and do the same procedure for all irreducible components of Q, we find that
the number of nodes cut out by Q1 is exactly 4e as the sum of all e equals to
(Q . H̃) = 6 and Q cuts out 4 × 6 = 24 nodes. This also shows that the vertex v
of V cannot be a smooth point of Y ; otherwise [End98, Proposition 2.4] would
imply that Q1 cuts out 4e + 1 nodes of Y .
Let Q1,F2 ⊂ F2 be the proper transform of Q1 . We claim that Q1,F2 and Q̃1

possibly at v. Hence, blowing up at {P1 , . . . , P65 } \ {v} does not affect both Q and
V . So, in the case v ̸∈ Y the claim is clear. If v ∈ Y , then v is a node of Y , so the
proper transform of V along P̃3 → P3 is nothing but the resolution F2 → V .

Corollary 194. Every nodal sextic Y with 65 nodes admits a pair (X, L)
where X ⊂ P6 is a nodal cubic with 32 nodes and L ⊂ X is a plane intersecting
Sing X at two points, such that Y is the discriminant of the projection pL : X 99K
P3 with centre L.
There is no such pair (X, L) with |X ∩ L| ≤ 1 such that Y is the discriminant
of the projection pL : X 99K P3 with centre L.
Proposition 195. Let X ⊂ P6 be a nodal cubic and let P1 , P2 be nodes of X.
Then the line Λ := P1 ∗ P2 is contained in X and there is a plane L containing
P1 , P2 such that L ⊂ X.
Proof. Λ · X ≥ 4, so Λ ⊂ X. Take coordinates
(u, x) := (u0 , u1 , x0 , . . . , x4 )
such that Λ = {x = 0} and such that the nodes are P1 = e0 , P2 = e1 .
Then the equation of X writes as
F (u, x) = u20 λ0,0 (x) + 2u0 u1 λ0,1 (x) + u21 λ1,1 (x) + u0 Q0 (x) + u1 Q1 (x) + G(x).
Since e0 , e1 are nodes the linear parts of the Taylor development vanish at them,
hence λ0,0 (x) ≡ 0, λ1,1 (x) ≡ 0, hence
F (u, x) = 2u0 u1 λ0,1 (x) + u0 Q0 (x) + u1 Q1 (x) + G(x).
Let Lx := Λ ∗ {(0, x)}: then Lx ⊂ X if and only if
λ0,1 (x) = Q0 (x) = Q1 (x) = G(x) = 0.

4.2. DISCRIMINANT NODAL SEXTIC SURFACES

165

These are 4 equations in P4 , so they always have a solution (and in general 12
solutions).
□

And L ⊂ X if and only if λ = 0.
In particular if we have an equisingular family X of nodal cubics Xt ⊂ P6 , and
we let Lt be the plane spanned by 3 nodes P1 (t), P2 (t), P3 (t) of Xt , then Lt ⊂ Xt
if and only if λ(t) = 0, hence we get a subfamily of codimension at most 1.
We can now summarize the previous Corollaries 190 and 194 in the following
Theorem 197. The Barth’s sextic as well as every other sextic surface with
65 nodes
i) is the discriminant of the projection pL of a cubic hypersurface X ⊂ P6
with 31 nodes, and with centre a plane L intersecting Sing(X) in three
points and not contained in a three dimensional linear subspace Λ ⊂ X,
ii) is the discriminant of the projection pL of a cubic hypersurface X ⊂ P6
with 32 nodes, and with centre a plane L intersecting Sing(X) in two
points and not contained in a three dimensional linear subspace Λ ⊂ X,
iii) cannot be the discriminant of the projection pL of a cubic hypersurface
X ⊂ P6 with 33 or more nodes.
F(6, 65) is smooth of dimension 18 at the point corresponding to the Barth
sextic, and at any other point corresponding to an unobstructed surface.
Taking cubic discriminants of cubic hypersurfaces as in i) or ii) one obtains
irreducible components of dimension ≥ 18 of F(6, 65).
Proof. (i) : by proposition 185 it suffices to show the existence of three
distinct half-even sets of nodes Ni , i = 1, 2, 3 of cardinality 15 such that N :=
N1 + N2 + N3 has cardinality t = 31.
We use the theorem proven in appendix B, that all sextic surfaces with 65 nodes
have extended code isomorphic to the one of the Barth sextic. Then it suffices to
show that the assertion holds for the Barth sextic.
While assertions (ii) and (iii) follow from Theorem 186 and Corollary 194.
That the Barth sextic is unobstructed is shown in Subsection 2.5.2, while an
explicit determinantal equation for the Barth sextic is contained in Section 4.4: in
this case L ∩ Sing(X) consists of three points since the cubic M = {det(A′ ) = 0}

166

4. NODAL SEXTICS

clearly consists of three planes, and we can then invoke Theorem 186 and Theorem
114.
For the assertion about the dimension, this follows easily from the fact that
the dimension of the space of sextics with 65 nodes is at least 83 − 65 = 18.
The smoothness follows from assertion vii) of theorem 38 that the Barth sextic is
unobstructed, which shows indeed that at this point the dimension of F(6, 65) is
exactly 18.
We can use the unobstructedness of the Segre cubic X0 (see v) of theorem
38) in order to obtain an irreducible component X32 ⊂ F5 (3, 32) with number
of moduli m = 3 and an irreducible component X31 ⊂ F5 (3, 31) with number of
moduli m = 4.
We can now take two nodes P1 (t), P2 (t) of Xt ∈ X32 and use proposition 195
to infer the existence of a plane Lt ⊂ Xt passing through P1 (t), P2 (t); in this way
we construct a family of dimension at least 18 of sextics which, by Theorem 114,
are 65-nodal if their singularities are just nodes.
Similarly, choosing three non collinear nodes of Xt′ ∈ X32 , we obtain, on a
subfamily of X32 of codimension at most 1, by remark 196, that the plane L′
spanned by the 3 nodes is contained in the cubic hypersurface Xt′ .
In this way we also construct a family of dimension at least 18 of sextics which,
by Theorem 114, are 65-nodal if their singularities are just nodes.
□
Remark 198. The previous theorem raises the question about the irreducibility of F(6, 65), in possible analogy with the irreducibility of F(3, 4), F(4, 16),
F(5, 31).
It also raises the question: given a general 32-nodal cubic X and two nodes,
is there only a finite number of planes contained in X and passing through the 2
nodes? Then the above construction would yield a family of dimension exactly 18.
Observe that up to now there are four different constructions of a 65-nodal
sextic: the Barth equation, the construction by Pettersen [Pet98] via nodal determinantal quartic hypersurfaces in P4 , and the above two constructions via cubic
discriminants.

4.2.3. Nodal sextics as quartic discriminants. Let W ⊂ P4 be a nodal
quartic hypersurface, and choose coordinates (w, x) := (w, x0 , . . . , x3 ) such that
(1, 0) is a node P0 of W = {f (w, x) = 0}. Then the equation of W writes as
f (w, x) = w2 Q(x) + wB(x) + g(x).
Projecting to P3 with centre the node P0 , we get the discriminant surface
Y = {x|F (x) := 4Q(x)g(x) − B 2 (x) = 0},
which is the branch locus of the rational double covering W 99K P3 .

4.2. DISCRIMINANT NODAL SEXTIC SURFACES

167

Comparing Sing(W ) with Sing(Y ) we see that
Sing(W ) = {(w, x)|2wQ + B = 0, w2 ∇Q + w∇B + ∇g = 0},
Sing(Y ) = {x|4g∇Q + 4Q∇g − 2B∇B = 0}.
One observes that if P is a singular point of W , then the line P0 ∗ P is either
contained in W , or it intersects W only in P0 , P , hence P maps to a point of Y .
The former situation occurs precisely when Q(x) = g(x) = B(x) = 0.
If Q(x) ̸= 0, then we have at most one singular point P of W on the line
B
P0 ∗ (0, x), which satisfies w = − 2Q
and then we see that the equation of W at P
is the suspension of the one of Y at x, since
4Qf = (2wQ)2 +2(2wQ)B +4Qg = (2wQ+B)2 +(4Qg−B 2 ) = (2wQ+B)2 +F (x).
We observe that w′ := (2wQ + B) completes the affine x coordinates to a system
of local coordinates.
Hence over the open set Q(x) ̸= 0 we have a bijection between singular points
of W and singular points of Y , such that the equation of W is of the form (w′ )2 +
F (x) = 0.
In particular, we have a node x ∈ Y downstairs if and only if we have a node
B
of W , P = (− 2Q
, x) ∈ W upstairs.
If instead Q(x) = 0, and P is a singular point, then B(x) = 0 for P , and also
g(x) = 0 for P .
Similarly, if Q(x) = 0 and x ∈ Y , then B(x) = 0. Over the points for which
Q(x) = B(x) = 0, the equations for the singular points are then simplified to
w2 ∇Q + w∇B + ∇g = 0,
respectively
g∇Q = 0.
At this point it is crucial to use the hypothesis that P0 is a node, ensuring that
the quadric Q is a smooth quadric, so that Q = ∇Q = 0 has no solution except
P0 .
Hence the singular points of Y with Q = 0 are only the points x where Q =
B = g = 0, and over them we do not have any singular point of W if the three
gradients are linearly independent, a condition which is equivalent to the condition
that these points x are nodes of Y . Follows:
Proposition 199. If the sextic surface Y is the discriminant of the projection
of a quartic hypersurface from a node P0 , then outside the closed set Q(x) =
B(x) = 0 we have a bijection between the singular points P = (w, x) of W and the
singular points x ∈ Sing(Y ), such that the singularity at P is the suspension of the
one of Y at x.
In particular, if Y is nodal, then W is also nodal, and there is a bijection
between the singular points of W different from P0 and the singular points of Y
different from the 24 points {Q = B = g = 0}.

168

4. NODAL SEXTICS

In particular, if Y has ν nodes, then W has δ = ν − 23 nodes.
Conversely, if W is nodal, again there is a bijection between the nodes of W
different from P0 and the singular points of Y which are not in the set {Q = B =
g = 0}, which are also nodes. While the points in the set {Q = B = g = 0} yield
nodes of Y if and only if the number of lines Λ passing through P0 and contained
in W is 24.
Proof. We have already proven the assertion concerning the points in the
open set Q(x) ̸= 0.
For the others, we use that since P0 is a node, the quadric Q is smooth. Then
we may restrict to consider the points lying in the closed set {Q = B = g = 0}.
If Y is nodal these are 24 nodes, moreover these are nodes if and only if this set
consists of 24 distinct points. This is however equivalent to requiring that on W
these equations define 24 different lines through P0 .
□
Pettersen [Pet98] considered quartics which are determinants of 4 × 4 matrices
with entries linear forms, which have in general 20 singular points (Proposition
1.3.7 ibidem).
With very hard (and also computer supported) work he was able to prove the
following result (Theorem 5.5.1 of [Pet98]):
Theorem 200. (Pettersen) There exists a unique irreducible 3-dimensional
family of 65-nodal sextic surfaces which are the discriminant of a nodal determinantal quartic hypersurface W with 42 nodes for the projection from one node.
This family contains the Barth sextic.
This theorem (taken cum grano salis replacing the word ‘dimension’ with ‘number of moduli’) produces then an irreducible family of dimension 18 of 65-nodal
sextics.
Conversely, we have:
Theorem 201. Each 65-nodal sextic Y admits an even set N of cardinality
24. N is symmetric hence determines a realization of Y as the discriminant of a
quartic hypersurface W for the projection with centre a double point P0 .
W has 42 nodes if and only if the unique quadric Q cuttingPon Ỹ the linear
system |L + H| is smooth (L is here the divisor such that 2L ≡ x∈N Ex ).
There exist choices of N such that either the rank of Q is either 3, or Q is
smooth.
In the case where Q has rank 3, P0 is not a node, and W has as singular points
P0 and 40 nodes, plus possibly another singular point P ′ .
The open set U inside the family of all 65-nodal sextics, consisting of the sextics admitting an even set N of cardinality 24 with Q smooth, and for which the
corresponding 42-nodal quartic hypersurface W is determinantal, is irreducible of
dimension 18 and contains the Barth sextic.

4.2. DISCRIMINANT NODAL SEXTIC SURFACES

169

Proof. Since we have seen that all 65-nodal sextics have the same code (Appendix C), there is an even set N of cardinality 24.
We have already seen in this section that we may assume that N is not the
sum of two half-even sets (of cardinality 15).
N is symmetric by Theorem 3.8.2 of [CC97], hence is associated to a representation
Y = {x|4Q(x)g(x) − B 2 (x) = 0},
where the quadric {Q(x) = 0} is uniquely defined. We may write Q = QN to
recall that the quadric depends upon the choice of N .
Observe that Q = QN is irreducible, else it is the union of two planes, and N
is the sum of two half-even sets of cardinality 15.
Since N = {Q = B = g = 0} we conclude that, if {Q(x) = 0} is smooth, then,
by Proposition 199, Y is the discriminant of a quartic W with 42 nodes.
The condition that {Q(x) = 0} is smooth is disc(Q) ̸= 0, hence defines an
open set U′ := {Y |∃ N s.t. disc(QN ) ̸= 0} inside the family of all 65-nodal sextics.
Moreover, the condition that W is determinantal is an open condition by virtue
of the following Lemma 202.
The rest follows then from Pettersen’s Theorem 200.
Finally, if {Q(x) = 0} is a rank 3 quadric, then, since we assume Y nodal, a
point x′ where Q = B = ∇Q = 0 would be a double point of Y , hence a node of
Y where g ̸= 0.
Then on the line P0 ∗ (0, x′ ) the singular points of W different from P0 are
determined by the equation w∇B + ∇g = 0. There is then at most one solution
P ′ , since ∇g = 0 implies g = 0, a contradiction.
If there is no such singular point x′ , then W shall have 41 nodes plus the
singular point P0 .
If such a point x′ exists, then W shall have 40 nodes plus the singular point
P0 plus possibly another singular point P ′ on the line P0 ∗ (0, x′ ).
□
The following result is indeed valid in a higher generality, but we just state in
a form suitable to our purposes here.
Lemma 202. Let W ⊂ P4 be a linear determinantal quartic W = {x|det(aij (x) =
0}, which has only δ nodes as singularities. Then there exists an open set of the
nodal Severi variety F3 (4, δ) containing W and consisting of nodal linear determinantal quartics.

4.2.4. Segre type representation of 65-nodal sextics. We shall now show
that if Y has 65 nodes, then it admits a Segre realization
Y = {λ1 (x) . . . λ6 (x) − B(x)2 = 0}.
This follows immediately from Theorem 42 once we prove that the Barth sextic
(hence, any other 65 nodal sextic) admits a subcode of K′′ which is the Segre code.
This in turn follows once we find a Segre realization of the Barth sextic: but
this is precisely given by the defining equation
1
(τ 2 x2 − y 2 )(τ 2 y 2 − z 2 )(τ 2 z 2 − x2 ) − (2τ + 1)w2 (x2 + y 2 + z 2 − w2 )2 ,
4
√
where τ := 21 (1 + 5) is the inverse of the golden ratio.
Here the six linear forms λi (x) are not in general position, since they have a
common zero set, but the code is indeed the Segre code since the 15 lines intersection of pairs of planes {λi (x) = λj (x) = 0} pass through 3 nodes each.

4.2. DISCRIMINANT NODAL SEXTIC SURFACES

171

We may ask whether there is a realization like for the Barth sextic:
Y = {x|λ1 (x) . . . λ6 (x) − λ0 (x)2 Q(x)2 = 0}.
Since Y has the same code K′ as the Barth sextic, we saw that there are
codewords v1 , . . . , v6 , such that they correspond to half-even sets of cardinality 15
produced by linear forms λ1 (x), . . . , λ6 (x).
The question boils down to the question whether the divisor of B contains the
divisor of a linear form λ0 , equivalently, whether B(x) = λi (x) = 0 defines a plane
cubic that splits as a conic and a line Λi , and the six lines Λ1 , . . . , Λ6 are coplanar.
However it seems that the above realization might be a special occurrence, by
virtue of the following observation.
It can be computationally checked2 that the extended code of Barth sextic
contains precisely 65 Segre subcodes. This leads to further 64 Segre realizations.
For instance, one of the realizations consists of six planes
(τ w−τ 2 x+z)(−τ w−τ 2 x+z)(−w+x−y+z)(w+x−y+z)(−w+x+y+z)(w+x+y+z),
where B(x) equals
τ 2 x3 − τ 2 w2 x − τ̄ xy 2 − w2 z + τ 3 x2 z − τ 3 y 2 z + τ̄ xz 2 + z 3 .3
In this case B(x) is an irreducible cubic, unlike the Segre realization introduced in
the beginning. We also remark that in the latter realization the six planes linearly
span P4 . This happens for every other Segre realization except for the first one.
It follows from this computational result that the group of projective automorphisms of the Barth surface fixes the point {x = y = z = 0}, leaves invariant the
plane {w = 0} and the sphere Σ := {x2 + y 2 + z 2 − w2 = 0}, and permutes the six
planes
{τ x ± y = 0}, {τ y ± z = 0}, {τ z ± x = 0}.
We use this remark in order to give a computational proof of the following
result
Theorem 203. The group of automorphisms of the Barth sextic Y has order
120, hence it is the Icosahedral group Z/2 × A5 .
We first observe that all automorphisms are projective automorphisms since
they preserve the canonical divisor, which is equal to 2H. Since the surface Y has
no two torsion in second homology, these automorphisms preserve H, hence they
are induced from projectivities.
The following argument is due to Stephen Coughlan.
2see the script [CCFK22, Mathematica 20].
3see the script [CCFK22, Mathematica 21].

We start from the defining equation f (w, x, y, z) = 0 of the Barth sextic Y ⊂
P3 , where F (w, x, y, z) :=
1

1
and τ := 2 (1 + 5) is the inverse of the golden ratio.
Using this observation, we show that the projective automorphism group of Y
has order 120, with the help of a computer.
First change the coordinates to
a := τ x − y, b := τ y − z, c := τ z − x, w := w,
and define expressions
d := τ (a + b) + c, e := τ (b + c) + a, f := τ (c + a) + b.
In these coordinates, the Barth sextic has the nice form
abcdef − 1/4τ w2 (ad + be + cf − τ w2 )2 ,
the six permuted planes are
(26)

{a = 0}, {b = 0}, {c = 0}, {d = 0}, {e = 0}, {f = 0}

and the invariant sphere is {ad + be + cf − τ w2 }.
In order to make the proof of Theorem 203 more transparent, we make its
statement more explicit.
Proposition 204. The projective automorphism group of the Barth sextic has
120 elements. Moreover, in the coordinates a, b, c, w, a projective automorphism
preserving the Barth sextic can be written in the form
w 7→ ±w, a 7→ p1 σ1 , b 7→ p2 σ2 , c 7→ p3 σ3
where (σ1 , σ2 , σ3 ) are certain triples of three distinct elements from {a, b, c, d, e, f },
and (p1 , p2 , p3 ) are certain triples in {±1}3 .
Proof. Let Φ be a projective automorphism of the Barth sextic. There exist
scalars α, β, γ, δ, ϵ, φ such that
Φ(a) = ασ1 , Φ(b) = βσ2 , Φ(c) = γσ3 , Φ(d) = δσ4 , Φ(e) = ϵσ5 , Φ(f ) = φσ6 .
where σ = (σ1 , . . . , σ6 ) is an ordered permutation of a, . . . , f .
Since Φ is a homomorphism, we have Φ(d) = Φ(τ (a + b) + c) and similar for
Φ(e), Φ(f ). Substituting the above general form for Φ into these equalities gives
three equations
δσ4 = τ (ασ1 + βσ2 ) + γσ3 , ϵσ5 = τ (βσ2 + γσ3 ) + ασ1 , φσ6 = τ (γσ3 + ασ1 ) + βσ2 .
Comparing the coefficients of a, b, c in each of the three equalities gives a homogeneous system of nine linear equations in six unknowns α, . . . , φ.
Using a computer, we consider each 6-tuple σ, construct its system of linear equations, and determine the space Vσ of solutions. We find that there are

4.3. THE CODE OF THE BARTH SEXTIC

173

60 permutations σ for which Vσ is spanned by a single vector in {±1}6 ; for the
other permutations, Vσ is the zero space and therefore does not correspond to an
automorphism.
We examine the cases where Vσ is nonempty and write p = (p1 , . . . , p6 ) for the
basis vector in {±1}. We have a 1-parameter family of possible automorphisms
corresponding to the space Vσ :
Φ(a) = p1 ασ1 , Φ(b) = p2 ασ2 , Φ(c) = p3 ασ3 ,
Φ(d) = p4 ασ4 , Φ(e) = p5 ασ5 , Φ(f ) = p6 ασ6 .
Next observe that the sphere ad + be + cf − τ w2 is invariant under Φ, and
Φ(w) = λw. Substituting this 1-parameter family and then scaling λ = ±α, we
get that Φ(ad + be + cf − τ w2 ) = λ2 (ad + be + cf − τ w2 ). Thus there are 60
permutations σ with Vσ non-zero, and for each such Vσ there are two possibilities
for λ, this gives 120 automorphisms in total. It is easy to see from the normal
form, that in each case, the Barth sextic is also invariant under Φ.
□
The proof uses a computer to work out the solutions of the system of linear
equations for each of the 720 permutations.
4.3. The code of the Barth sextic
We start again from the defining equation f (w, x, y, z) = 0 of the Barth sextic
Y ⊂ P3 , where f (w, x, y, z) :=
1
(τ 2 x2 − y 2 )(τ 2 y 2 − z 2 )(τ 2 z 2 − x2 ) − (2τ + 1)w2 (x2 + y 2 + z 2 − w2 )2

and τ := 2 (1 + 5) is the inverse of the golden ratio.
In [Bar96], Barth described the icosahedral symmetry of Y . Consider the
icosahedron with vertices
(1, 0, ±τ, ±1), (1, ±1, 0, ±τ ), (1, ±τ, ±1, 0).
Each face of the icosahedron can be labelled by an ordered pair of distinct integers
ij, 1 ≤ i, j ≤ 5. Figure 1 shows the labelling using the dual dodecahedron.
The action of A5 via σ · ij 7→ σ(i)σ(j) realizes A5 as the group of rotational
icosahedral symmetries.
Consider a line which connects two opposite centres of the faces of the icosahedron. Such a line is called a centre line. In Figure 1, centre lines are the lines
connecting opposite vertices, so they can be labelled by the unordered pair (ij).
The parentheses mean that we do not distinguish (ij) and (ji). The mid lines
are the lines which connect mid points of two opposite edges of the icosahedron.
Equivalently, they are the lines connecting mid points of the opposite edges of the
dual dodecahedron. They correspond to double transpositions, hence they can be
labelled by (ij)(kl), where i, j, k, l are all distinct.

Each mid line contains 3 nodes, and each centre line contains 2 nodes. Since
there are 15 mid lines and 10 centre lines, all the 65 = 3 × 15 + 2 × 10 nodes belong
to these lines.
(A,B) Each mid line (ij)(kl) intersects the plane (w = 0) at a node, which will
be denoted by A(ij)(kl) . In the affine space (w ̸= 0), the node closer to ij
will be denoted by Bij(kl) .
(C) For each centre line (ij), the node close to ij will be denoted by Cij .
The nodes of type A form an orbit of A5 , they correspond to double pairs
(stabilised by a Klein subgroup (Z/2)2 ), that is, double transpositions.
Similarly those of type C, form the orbit of ordered pairs, and are stabilised
by a 3-cycle. These correspond to cyclic subgroups of order 3 (self normalizing).
Those of type B form the orbit stabilised by a double transposition: one takes
an ordered pair and a pair (in this way we get an orbit of 60 elements on which

4.3. THE CODE OF THE BARTH SEXTIC

175

A5 acts transitively), but then identifying ab(cd) with ba(dc) the stabiliser becomes (ab)(cd), hence we get the cosets for the subgroup generated by this double
transposition.
Clearly, these are three orbits of the A5 -action on the set of nodes.
