Since we know from e.g. [JR97] that dim(K) ≤ 12, the above matrix is a
generator matrix of K′ and we obtain a generator matrix of K by removing the
first row and the first column5.
The weight enumerator 6 of K is given by
W (x) = 1x0 + 390x24 + 3055x32 + 650x40
and the weight enumerator of K′ \K is given by
W (x) = 26x16 + 650x28 + 1690x32 + 1300x36 + 300x40 + 130x44 .
Both codes K and K′ have an automorphism group of order 15 600 = 24 · 3 · 52 · 13
that is isomorphic to PΣL(2, 25) = PSL(2, 25) ⋊ ⟨F ⟩, where F : x 7→ x5 is the
Frobenius automorphism of F25 , as can be checked by a MAGMA computation 7
and for reasons that we shall now explain.
In fact, we can associate to Y a graph G with the same automorphism group.
To this end let P1 , . . . , P65 be the vertices of G. Two vertices Pi and Pj are joined
by an edge if and only if
gi,j (t) := f (t · Pi + (1 − t) · Pj ) = ct2 (1 − t)2 (t − x1 )(t − x2 ),
where c, x1 , x2 ∈ C and |{0, 1, x1 , x2 }| = 4, i.e., the line t · Pi + (1 − t) · Pj intersects
the surface Y in the two nodes Pi , Pj and two different additional points.
5Another argument for the fact that the above matrix M is a generator matrix of K′ , without

using the result of [JR97], goes as follows. By construction, removing the first row and column
generates a subcode of K. It can be easily checked that this subcode is projective, so that we
can conclude from Proposition 225 that no extension to a 13-dimensional code exists.
6
see the script [CCFK22, Mathematica 4].
7see the script [CCFK22, MAGMA 22].

4.3. THE CODE OF THE BARTH SEXTIC

179

Every linear automorphism ρ preserving the equation f corresponds to an
automorphism ρ̃ of K, i.e., ρ induces a permutations on Sing(Y ), which can be
written as a permutation ρ̃ of the columns of a generator matrix of K. The converse
is not true in general.
In subsection 4.3.1, we shall prove that the group of linear automorphisms of the
Barth surface is indeed A5 , (Z/2)3 , where (Z/2)3 is the group of the coordinate
sign changes, and has order 120.
In particular the Barth surface does not admit a linear automorphism of order
13, while the automorphism group of K contains elements of order 13.
The graph G is nowadays called Doro-Hall graph and is one of the three locally
Petersen graphs, i.e., graphs where the neighbourhood of every vertex is isomorphic to the Petersen graph (quotients of the dodecahedral graph by the antipodal
involution), see [Hal80].
4.3.1. The automorphism group of the Barth sextic. We know that the
set N of the 65 nodes is invariant under a group of linear collineations isomorphic
to the icosahedral group A5 × Z/2Z.
The aim of this subsection is to given a non computational proof of Theorem
203, that the icosahedral group A5 × Z/2Z is the full group of automorphisms of
the Barth sextic.
The action of A5 partitions N into three orbits NA , NB and NC , which are the
sets of nodes of type A, B and C of size 15, 30 and 20, respectively.
We call a line L a node-secant if it contains at least 2 nodes. By abuse of
notation we shall say that L is a secant. If L contains exactly k nodes, L is called
a k-secant.
4.3.1.1. Secants through NA . The nodes of type A span the plane (w = 0) =: E.
Throughout 4.3.1.1, we shall only consider the nodes and secants inside E. As
w = 0 for all points in E, the w-coordinate is skipped in computations.
Lemma 206. Let P ∈ NA .
(a) The node P is contained in precisely two 2-secants, two 3-secants and two
5-secants.
(b) Let Q, Q′ ∈ NA be the second nodes on the two 2-secants through P . Then
QQ′ is a 2-secant.
Proof. As the nodes of type A form a single orbit under the A5 -action, it is
enough to consider the single node P = (1, 0, 0). For part (a), we compute the
list of secants through P shown in Table 2. For part (b), we see that {Q, Q′ } =
{(0, 1, 0), (0, 0, 1)}, so QQ′ = (x = 0), which contains no other node of type A. □
In the next steps, we will avoid doing direct coordinate computations. Instead,
the conclusions will solely be drawn from the incidence properties of the secants
provided by Lemma 206. The exception will be the final Lemma 215, where the
incidence properties are not enough to show the statement, see Remark 216.

Lemma 207. The secants of NA fall into fifteen 2-secants, ten 3-secants and
six 5-secants.
Proof. Use Lemma 206 and count the flags (P, L) with P ∈ NA , L a secant
and P ∈ L in two ways.
□
By Lemma 206(b), the 2-secants partition NA into five parts of size 3, which
we will call triads. Let T be the set of the five triads. For P ∈ NA , let TP be the
triad containing P .
Remark 208. In the proof of Lemma 206(b), we have seen that the three nodes
A(23)(45) , A(25)(34) and A(24)(53) form a triad, which are exactly the nodes of type A
where the number 1 does not appear in the label. Since by construction, the A5 action is compatible with the labelling, we get that the five triads Ti with {1, . . . , 5}
are given by the five nodes of type A where the number i does not appear in the
label.
Lemma 209.
(a) Each 5-secant contains exactly one node of every triad.
(b) For each P ∈ NA , the two 3-secants passing through P together contain
exactly one node from each triad different from TP .
Proof. Any pair of nodes in the same triad spans a 2-secant, so no 3- or
5-secant can contain two nodes from the same triad. This implies part (a).
For part (b), let P ∈ NA and let T be one of the four triads different from
TP . For each Q ∈ T , the line P Q is a 3-secant or a 5-secant. By part (a) and
since there are two 5-secants passing through P , for two of the three nodes Q ∈ T

4.3. THE CODE OF THE BARTH SEXTIC

181

the line P Q is a 5-secant. Therefore, for the third node Q ∈ T the line P Q is a
3-secant.
□
Lemma 210. Let T1 , T2 , T3 be three different triads. Then there is a unique
3-secant containing a node from each triad T1 , T2 , T3 .
Proof. We denote the other two triads by T4 and T5 . Assume that there are
two 3-secants L1 , L2 containing a node from each triad T1 , T2 , T3 . By Lemma 209(b),
L1 ∩ L2 is not a node, so L1 and L2 together cover 6 distinct nodes. Denote them
by Pij with Pij ∈ Lj , Pij ∈ Ti where i ∈ {1, 2, 3} and j ∈ {1, 2}. Each node Pij
is contained in a second 3-secant Lij . By Lemma 209(b), the other two nodes on
Lij are contained in T4 and T5 . In particular, the six 3-secants Lij are pairwise
distinct.
Assume that there is a node Q ∈ T4 ∪ T5 contained in two 3-secants Lij ̸= Li′ j ′
with i, i′ ∈ {1, 2, 3} and j, j ′ ∈ {1, 2}. Then Lij and Li′ j ′ are two distinct 3secants through Q both containing a node of T4 and T5 . This not possible by
Lemma 209(b), so the six 3-secants Lij cover 12 distinct nodes in T4 ∪ T5 . This is
a contradiction to #(T4 ∪ T5 ) = 6.
So there is at most one 3-secant containing a node of T1 , T2 and T3 . Now the
claim follows as the number of 3-secants equals the number 53 = 10 of 3-sets of
triads.
□
We will call a 2-set of two disjoint 2-sets of triads a tetrad and we will denote
the set of tetrads by X. Note that #X = 52 32 /2 = 15. A node P ∈ NA defines
the set {L1 , L2 } of the two 3-secants passing through P . For i ∈ {1, 2}, the 3secant Li determines the set {Ti1 , Ti2 } of the two triads different from TP which
contain a point on Li . By Lemma 209(b), the set
t(P ) := {{T11 , T12 }, {T21 , T22 }}
is a tetrad.
Example 211. Let T = {T1 , T2 , T3 , T4 , T5 } with Ti defined as in Remark 208
and let P = A(23)(45) = (1, 0, 0). From Table 2, the two 3-secants passing through
P are L1 = (y + τ 2 z = 0) containing the nodes (1, −τ, −τ̄ ) = A(31)(52) ∈ T4 and
(1, τ, τ̄ ) = A(51)(34) ∈ T2 and L2 = (y − τ 2 z = 0) containing the nodes (1, τ, −τ̄ ) =
A(12)(34) ∈ T5 and (−1, τ, −τ̄ ) = A(41)(52) ∈ T3 . Therefore, t(A(23)(45) ) = {{T4 , T2 }, {T5 , T3 }}.
This example shows that to our surprise, the map t does not match the labelling
of the nodes of type A introduced in the construction.
Lemma 212. The map t : NP → X is a bijection.
Proof. By #X = 15 = #NA , it suffices to show that t is injective. Let
P ∈ NA and t(P ) = {{T1 , T2 }, {T3 , T4 }} ∈ X. We denote the fifth triad by T5 . By
Lemma 210, there is a unique 3-secant L passing through nodes on T1 , T2 and T5 ,
so P is determined as the node L ∩ T5 .
□

Now let GA be the stabilizer of the point set NA in Aut(E). The map t induces
a map tG : GA → S(X).
Lemma 213. The map tG : GA → S(X) is an injective homomorphism of
groups.
Proof. As t is defined based on incidence properties of lines spanned by the
nodes in NA , the map tG is a homomorphism of groups. Let g ∈ GA and let φ =
tG (g). For P ∈ NA , g(P ) is uniquely determined as t−1 (φ(t(P ))) by Lemma 212.
Therefore g is uniquely determined by φ = tG (g) and thus tG is an injection. □
Remark 214. We can use Lemma 213 to efficiently complete Example 211 to
a list of all t-values of the nodes of type A. From Example 211 we know that
t(1, 0, 0) = t(A(23)(45) ) = {{T2 , T4 }, {T3 , T5 }}.
Since t is a group homomorphism and the A5 -action is compatible with the labelling
of the nodes of type A, for σ = (234) ∈ A5 we get that
t(0, 1, 0) = t(A(34)(25) ) = t(σ · A(23)(45) ) = σ(t(A(23)(45) ))
= σ({{T2 , T4 }, {T3 , T5 }}) = {{T2 , T3 }, {T4 , T5 }}.
Using other elements σ ∈ A5 , we get the list in Table 3. For completeness, we also
included the list of the triads of the nodes.

= A5 .
Proof. Let T = {T1 , T2 , T3 , T4 , T5 } with Ti defined as in Remark 208. Assume
that there is a ρ ∈ GA such that tG (ρ) is the transposition T3 ↔ T4 . From Table 3
we get that
ρ(1, 0, 0) = (0, 1, 0), ρ(0, 1, 0) = (1, 0, 0) and ρ(0, 0, 1) = (0, 0, 1).
Therefore, ρ can be represented by a matrix

Now one checks that S does not map (1, τ, τ̄ ) to a scalar multiple of (τ̄ , 1, τ ), which
is a contradiction to the second condition.

Remark 216. It can be checked that the set of permutations of NA preserving
the incidences among all secants is indeed isomorphic to the full symmetric group
S5 . For that reason, a proof of Lemma 215 must involve some additional property,
like in the above proof the concrete coordinate representation of the nodes of type
A.
4.3.1.2. The automorphisms of the Barth sextic. Let G be the stabilizer of N
in PGL(C4 ).
Lemma 217. The 5-secants in Lemma 207 are the only secants that pass through
more than four nodes. In particular, G preserves the nodes of type A.
Proof. Since nodes of type B are in the conic w2 = x2 + y 2 + z 2 , no line
contains more than two of them. The same is true for nodes of type C since they
are contained in 3w2 = x2 + y 2 + z 2 . Thus, if a 5-secant L is not contained in E,
then it contains precisely one node of type A, two nodes of type B, and two nodes of

184

4. NODAL SEXTICS

type C. Using A5 -symmetry, we may assume that this node is A(23)(45) = (0, 1, 0, 0).
Then, L can be parametrized by t 7→ (1, t, y0 , z0 ), but it can be directly checked
from Table 1 that nodes of type B and C are distinguishable by looking at yzcoordinates. Thus, L cannot join nodes of type B and of type C. Now, it is obvious
that a k-secant (k ≥ 5) contains at least two nodes of type A, hence k = 5.
The last statement follows from the fact that the 5-secants in E covers NA . □

By Lemma 218 we will call the point O the centre of the Barth sextic.
Lemma 219. The secants through the centre O consist of fifteen 3-secants of
type pattern ABB8 and ten 2-secants of type pattern CC.
Proof. It can be immediately seen that the line joining O and A(ij)(kl) contains
Bij(kl) and Bji(lk) . A line joining O and Cij also contains Cji . In both cases, the line
cannot contain further nodes as there is no 5-secants of type pattern ABBCC (cf.
Lemma 217 and its proof).

Remark 220. Let U ⊂ P3 be the union of non-secant lines which intersect N .
In the proof of Lemma 219, we observed that O ̸∈ U . In fact, a computer-based
approach9 verifies that U = P3 \ {O}. To verify, we compute the intersections of
all the pairs of secants, and for each intersection point, find the subset of N that
are covered by secants passing though this point. The centre is the unique point
through which the secants cover the entire N .
This is another interesting property that characterizes the centre.
Lemma 221. The elements of G preserve the type of the nodes.
Proof. By Lemma 217, G preserves the nodes of type A. Also, by Lemmas 218
and 219, G preserves the set {Cij Cji }. This shows that G preserves the nodes of
type C, and then also the nodes of type B.
□
Proposition 222. The group of linear automorphisms of the set of nodes of
the Barth sextic is isomorphic to the icosahedral group A5 × Z/2Z.
Proof. By Lemma 221, NA (and hence the plane E spanned by NA ) is invariant under the elements of G. Therefore, the group homomorphism Φ : G → GE ,
ϕ 7→ ϕ|E is well-defined. By Lemma 215 we have Im Φ ≤ GE ∼
= A5 , so # Im Φ ≤ 60.
Let ϕ ∈ ker Φ. Then ϕ fixes all the points in E. Moreover, the centre O ∈
/ E is
fixed by Lemma 218. Now as an element of PGL, the collineation ϕ is determined
by the image of a single further point P ∈
/ (E ∪ {O}). We choose the node
P = (1, 1, 0, 0) of type B. The line L = OP is a secant of type pattern ABB (see
Lemma 219). As the single node Q of type A is fixed by ϕ ∈ ker Φ, we get that
ϕ(L) = ϕ(OQ) = OQ = L. Therefore, the node P of type B is mapped to one of
the two nodes of type B on L, implying that # ker Φ ≤ 2.
Now by the fundamental theorem on homomorphisms,
#G = # ker Φ · # Im Φ ≤ 2 · 60 = 120.
This finishes the proof as we already know that G contains a subgroup isomorphic
to the icosahedral group of order 120.
□
4.3.2. The Doro-Hall graph.
4.3.2.1. Petersen graph. As we already mentioned, the Petersen graph is constructed by the vertices and edges of the projective dodecahedron, that is, a dodecahedron where opposite points, lines and faces are identified with each other.
As we have also seen, the Petersen graph can also be constructed as the particular Kneser graph with n = 5 and k = 2.
Recall that the Kneser graph with indices n, k is formed from a set X with
n elements, taking as vertex set V the set of subsets of cardinality k of X, and
joining two vertices with an edge if and only if the corresponding sets are disjoint.
We denote by E the set of edges.
9see the script [CCFK22, MAGMA 22].

186

4. NODAL SEXTICS

The number of vertices of the Petersen graph is then 10 and the number of
edges is 15.
The 45 (unordered) pairs of vertices fall into 15 at distance 1 (edges) and 30
at distance 2, in particular the Petersen graph has diameter 2.
The Petersen graph is the unique strongly regular graph with the parameters
v = 10, k = 3, λ = 0 and µ = 1. This means that the Petersen graph has v = 10
vertices, each vertex has k = 3 neighbours. For every neighbouring pair of vertices,
there are λ = 0 common neighbours, and for any non-neighbouring pair or vertices,
there is µ = 1 common neighbour.
Moreover, the Petersen graph has diameter 2, and since its automorphism group
is S5 , one sees easily that it is distance-transitive (that is, it acts transitively on
the ordered pairs of vertices having a fixed distance).
The girth (the minimal length of a cycle) of the Petersen graph is 5. Each edge
is contained in four 5-cycles, and the total number of 5-cycles is 12.
The independence number (maximum number of a set of vertices not connected
by an edge) of the Petersen graph is 4. It has 5 maximal independent sets, which
in the Kneser representation are given by the sets of 4 unordered pairs containing
a common element x ∈ X. Let us denote by Ix the independent set corresponding
to x ∈ X; hence the set of all 5 independent sets I is in bijection with X. The
stabilizer of any maximal independent set is therefore S4 (the stabilizer of x in
S5 ).
Each vertex a = {x, y} is contained in two maximal independent sets, namely
Ix and Iy . In a maximal independent set, any pair of vertices is at distance 2.
Each pair of vertices a, b of the Petersen graph with d(a, b) = 2 is contained in a
unique maximal independent set. 10
4.3.2.2. Doro-Hall graph. In [Hal80], the connected local Petersen graphs (that
is, the graphs such that, for any vertex, the subgraph having as vertex set the set
of its neighbours is a Petersen graph) have been classified.
There are only three such graphs, having respectively 21, 63 and 65 vertices.
We shall refer to the largest of these graphs as the Doro-Hall graph (it is often
simply called the Hall-graph).
For an overview on the Doro-Hall graph, see [BCN89, Prop. 12.2.2] and the
subsequent paragraph.
In [Hal80, Thm. 1.2], it has been shown that there is a unique distance-regular
graph on 65 vertices with intersection array {10, 6, 4; 1, 2, 5}. In [Hal80], the
original discovery of this graph is attributed to a manuscript ”Two new distancetransitive graphs” by Stephen Doro, which apparently has never been published.
We call it the Doro-Hall graph − to acknowledge credit to the original discoverer. The graph has been rediscovered independently in [GL81].
10d(a, b) = 2 implies #(a ∩ b) = 1, so a ∩ b = {x} with x ∈ X and the unique maximal

independent set containing {a, b} is Ix .

4.3. THE CODE OF THE BARTH SEXTIC

187

By [GL81, Prop. 6], combined with the uniqueness result of [Hal80, Thm. 1.2],
the automorphism group of Γ is isomorphic to PΣL(2, 25). The graph Γ is distancetransitive, where already the normal index-2 subgroup PSL(2, 25) is acting transitively on all ordered pairs of vertices of fixed distance.
Moreover, Γ is a commuting involutions graph, meaning that, for any x ∈ V ,
there is a unique nontrivial automorphism τx (which necessarily is an involution)
of Γ pointwise stabilizing x and all neighbours of x, and moreover any two distinct
vertices of x, y ∈ V are neighbours if and only if τx and τy commute.
The following constructions of Γ are known.
• Let F : x 7→ x5 be the Frobenius automorphism of F25 .
Then G = PΣL(2, 25) = PSL(2, 25) ⋊ ⟨F ⟩ is a group of order 15600 =
24 · 3 · 52 · 13.
The set V of vertices of the Hall graph is the conjugacy class F G of F
in G. It is of size 65. The set E of edges is given by the 325 unordered
pairs of distinct commuting elements in F G . [Hal80]
• Let q ∈ F5 [x1 , x2 , x3 , x4 ] be a non-degenerate elliptic quadratic form11 and
Q = {⟨x⟩ ∈ P3F5 | q(x) = 0} the corresponding quadric.
The vertex set V is defined as the set of all points ⟨x⟩ ∈ P3F5 with
x ∈ F45 such that q(x) ∈ {±1}.
The edge set E is defined as the set of pairs {x, y} ⊂ V such that the
line L = x ∗ y is a secant of Q, i.e. L intersects Q in 2 distinct points
(that is, these points are in Q ⊂ P3F5 ). [BCN89, Sec. 12.2]
• An inversive plane of order 5 is a combinatorial 3-(26, 6, 1) design. The
set of 130 blocks is invariant under the action of PSL(2, 25), partitioning
the design in two orbits of size 65. Now let V be one of the orbits and
define two blocks in V to be neighbours if they are disjoint. [GL81]
For each vertex, the distance distribution of the other 64 vertices is (110 230 324 ).
4.3.2.3. From the Doro-Hall graph to the code K of the Barth sextic. The code
K of the Barth sextic is indeed isomorphic to the code associated to the Doro-Hall
graph as in the following Theorem 223: for each vertex, the characteristic function
of the set of 24 vertices at distance 3 yields a codeword in F65
2 of Hamming weight
24. The linear hull of these 65 codewords is the code K consisting of the (strictly)
even set of nodes of the Barth sextic. By construction, this code is invariant under
the action of the PΣL(2, 25) in the Doro construction of the Doro-Hall graph.
Theorem 223 shows also how K′ is obtained from the Doro-Hall graph; moreover in Appendix C we give another description of how K′ can be derived from
K by coding theoretic means. In particular, K′ is invariant under the action
of PΣL(2, 25) too, where the extra coordinate is always left constant. In fact,
PΣL(2, 25) is the full automorphism group of K as well as of K′ .

4.3.2.4. From the Barth sextic to the Doro-Hall graph. Indeed, the Doro-Hall
graph structure can also be seen from the geometry of the Barth sextic.
The following has been found computationally 12
By the A5 -action, the 65 nodes of the Barth surface YB are partitioned into 3
orbits of respective lengths 15 (Type A), 20 (Type B) and 30 (Type C). For the
line L connecting an ordered pair of nodes, x and y, of the Barth sextic, one of
the following possibilities occurs:
• Type X: L intersects YB in two further points.
• Type Y: L intersects YB in a further point with multiplicity 2.
• Type Z: x has multiplicity 4 and y has multiplicity 2 on L (Type Z+ ) or
the other way round (Type Z− ).
• Type 0: L is contained in YB .
The frequencies of the types of the lines passing through a node x depend on
the type of x. We get the following table:

Let us call two nodes neighbours if their connecting line is of type X: then we
obtain a graph, which is indeed isomorphic to the Doro-Hall graph 13.
We remark that there are exactly 6 lines connecting a pair of nodes which are
completely contained in YB . Every such line contains 5 nodes, all of type A. Each
node of type A is contained in two such lines.
We have the following synthetic description of the codes of the Barth sextic in
terms of the Doro-Hall graph:
Theorem 223. The projection K′′ of the extended code K′ of the Barth sextic
is isomorphic to the code generated by the characteristic functions of independent
sets of maximal cardinality of the Doro-Hall graph (a set N is independent if no
edge joins two vertices in N ).
The code K is generated by the sums of pairs of such characteristic functions
(which have weight 15): these sums yield 325 vectors of weight 24.
Another generating set for K is given by the other vectors of weight 24: these
are the 65 characteristic functions of the spheres of radius 3 in the Doro-Hall graph
G (set of vertices at distance 3 from a given vertex v).
Proof. We know that the extended code K′ is generated by the codewords
corresponding to the half-even sets of cardinality 15.
12see the script [CCFK22, MAGMA 23].
13see the script [CCFK22, Mathematica 10].

4.3. THE CODE OF THE BARTH SEXTIC

189

Observe that in a given half-even set of cardinality 15, the line connecting any
two nodes is not an edge of G.
This is because, setting x = 0 the equation of the corresponding plane, the
equation of f writes as f = xϕ + g32 , so that the plane section with {x = 0} is
the plane cubic curve {x = g = 0} counted with multiplicity 2, and the 15 nodes
are the intersections of the cubic curve with the quintic surface ϕ; hence each such
line intersects in three points.
So the corresponding vertices of G form an independent set (a subset of vertices
whose induced subgraph is edgeless) of cardinality 15.
It can be checked that these sets are independent sets of maximal cardinality,
and indeed every independent set of 15 vertices corresponds to a half-even set of
cardinality 15, as shown by the Mathematica built-in library about the (Doro-)
Hall graph, including the independence polynomial 14:
1 + 65x + 1755x2 + 25805x3 + 227890x4 + 1259583x5 + 4414930x6 +
+9762935x7 + 13342485x8 + 10860980x9 + 4966663x10 +
+1164540x11 + 120380x12 + 4980x13 + 390x14 + 26x15 ,
where the coefficient of xk is the number of independent sets consisting of k
vertices. In particular, 15 is the maximum cardinality of an independent set, and
there are only 26 of them, namely, the 26 half-even sets of cardinality 15. 15
We have seen that if N1 , N2 are half-even sets of cardinality 15, then their
intersection has cardinality 3 (the line intersection of the two planes meets the
surface in 3 points), hence the symmetric difference (N1 ∪ N2 ) \ (N1 ∩ N2 ) is a
strictly even set of cardinality 24. These are 21 26 · 25 = 325 vectors, because such
sets of cardinality 24 determine uniquely a couple of planes containing 15 nodes (in
fact, a line cannot contain 6 nodes of the sextic, otherwise each plane containing
the line would cut the line with mutiplicity 2, hence the line would be contained
in the singular set of Y ); these 325 vectors generate the code K.
Our knowledge of the weight enumerator for our code (see Appendix C) shows
that there are exactly 390 vectors of weight 24, and it can be verified via computer
calculations 16 that the remaining 65 are the characteristic functions of the spheres
of radius 3 and that they generate the code K. Observe in fact that, given a vertex
v of G, there are exactly 10 vertices at distance 1 from v, 30 at distance 2, and 24
at distance 3 (so 3 is the diameter of G).
□
Remark 224. I) The code K of the Barth sextic is an irreducible representation
of its symmetry group PΣL(2, 25).
In fact, let C be a subcode of K: if C contains a vector of weight 24, then
either it contains one of the 65 characteristic functions of a sphere of radius 3, or

it contains one of the 325 sums of pairs of characteristic functions of independent
sets of maximal cardinality. In both cases C would contain the whole orbit if C is
PΣL(2, 25)-invariant, hence we would have C = K.
There remains to exclude that C can only have the weights 32, 40.
PΣL(2, 25)-invariance implies that the effective length of C is 65, hence by
Bonisoli’s theorem it cannot be a one weight code (because none of 65, 13, 5 is a
power of 2 diminished by 1).
Therefore C would have to admit both weights 32, 40. If we use that the vectors
in K of weight 40 form a single orbit of cardinality 650 (this is again verified by
computer calculations 17), then the dimension of C is at least 10 and we can use
the Griesmer bound to compute n ≥ 67, a contradiction.
Another possibility, using Part II) of this remark, would be to exclude that C
cannot be one of the A5 -submodules U43 , U44 .

II) The inclusion K ⊂ VA ⊕ VB ⊕ VC produces six projections, and the kernels
are A5 -submodules.

these are irreducible A5 -submodules of dimension 4, while KB = 0.

4.4. Determinantal equations of the Barth sextic.
In this section we present an explicit description of Barth’s sextic Y ⊂ P3 as
a determinant of the matrix A whose diagonal degrees are (1, 1, 1, 3). In view
of corollary 174 and lemma 176, the first step is to find a symmetric half-even
set of cardinality 31. To do this, we consider the A5 -orbit of the linear form
ℓ1 := w − x − y − z:
ℓ1 = w − x − y − z

ℓ2 = w + x − y + z

ℓ3 = w − x + y + z

ℓ4 = w + x + y − z

ℓ5 = w + x + y + z

ℓ6 = τ w − τ 2 x + z

ℓ7 = τ̄ w − τ̄ 2 y + z

ℓ8 = τ̄ w − τ̄ 2 x + y

ℓ9 = w − x + y − z

ℓ10 = τ w + τ 2 x − z

ℓ11 = τ̄ + τ̄ 2 x + y

ℓ12 = τ̄ w − τ̄ 2 y − z

ℓ13 = w + x − y − z

ℓ14 = τ̄ w + τ̄ 2 y − z

ℓ15 = τ̄ w − τ̄ 2 x − y

ℓ16 = τ w − τ 2 x − z

ℓ17 = w − x − y + z

ℓ18 = τ̄ w + τ̄ 2 x − y

ℓ19 = τ̄ w + τ̄ 2 y + z

ℓ20 = τ w + τ 2 x + z.

instance, we may pick P = [2 : 1 : 1 : 1] ∈ Y .

sextic equation, and adj(Bij ) = (constant) · f 2 A, where A is the matrix

3
A final observation is that one could try to construct surfaces with many nodes
also in higher degree by taking discriminants of cubic hypersurfaces with sufficiently many nodes.
However, while as we saw for degree d = 4, 5 we get exactly nodal maximizing
surfaces by choosing a cubic hypersurface with the maximal number of nodes (
16 = 10 + 6, 31 = 15 + 16), we have shown here that we cannot take for degree
d = 6 such a cubic, since 35 + 31 = 66 > 65.
For d = 7 we would get 56 + 52 = 108 > 104, which is an upper bound for µ(7)
(see for instance [Lab05]), so we should take cubics with at most 48 nodes.
The case d = 8, since, as shown in lemma 99, for a cubic hypersurface in P8
the 4-dimensional linear subspaces L ⊂ X pass through some singular points of
X, shows the necessity of taking (as it happens already for sextics with 65 nodes)
subspaces passing through several nodes of X.

Appendix C. Codes of nodal sextics with many nodes
Sascha Kurz

The aim of this section is to determine the possible strict codes K for nodal
sextics in P3 with ν nodes, when ν is large, i.e., ν ∈ {64, 65}.

Clearly for the effective length n we have n ≤ ν and the B-inequality (remark 33) gives k ≥ ν − 53, i.e., k ≥ 12 for ν = 65 and k ≥ 11 for ν = 64.
The set of possible non-zero weights for the strict code K of a nodal sextic in
P3 is {24, 32, 40, 56}, see [CT05].

The codes of proposition 225 are the only possible candidates for the strict
code K of a nodal sextic in P3 with ν = 65 nodes, where the code in case (3)
is isomorphic to the Barth code KB . To further thin out this list we use known
properties of the extended code K′ , i.e., the minimum distance is at least 16 and
the weights are divisible by four, see e.g. [End98, Theorem 1.10]. Moreover, K′ \K
does not contain codewords of weight 20 or 24, see [End98, Corollary 1.11].

Proposition 225, respectively, see section 4.3 for their description.

Proof. Due to remark 33 the dimension of K is at least 12 and from [CT05]
we conclude that the non-zero weights in K are contained in {24, 32, 40, 56}. Thus
we can assume that K is isomorphic to one of the three cases listed in proposition 225. Note that the effective length is 62 + i in case (i), where 1 ≤ i ≤ 3, and
that the dimension is 12 in all cases.
From proposition 32 we conclude that the dimension of K′ is 13. The effective
length of K′ lies between 63 + i and 66. As shown in [End98] the weights of the
codewords in K′ \K are contained in
{16, 28, 32, 36, 40, 44, 48, 52, 56, 60, 64} =: W.
So, if K is isomorphic to the code in case (i) in proposition 225, then the
existence of the extended code K′ implies that the system of lemma 226 has a
feasible solution for at least one of the choices 1 ≤ δ ≤ 4 − i and Λ = γ ∈ W .
Using Cplex we computationally check that there are no feasible solutions except
when i = 3, δ = 1, and Λ = γ = 44. Thus, K is isomorphic to KB . For i = 3,
δ = 1, and Λ = γ = 44 we let Cplex enumerate all solutions. There are exactly

Proof. Due to remark 33 the dimension k of K is at least 11 and from [CT05]
we conclude that the non-zero weights in K are contained in {24, 32, 40, 56}. If
k ≥ 12, then we can read off the possibilities for K from proposition 225. The two
cases with effective length at most 64 are listed as cases (f) and (g).
For the remaining cases we can assume that k = 11. Using the software package
LinCode we enumerate all 357 codes with dimension 11, length 64, and non-zero
weights in {24, 32, 40, 56}. To sift out this list we consider the extended code K′ .
From proposition 32 we conclude that the dimension of K′ is 12. As shown in
[End98] the weights of the codewords in K′ \K are contained in
{16, 28, 32, 36, 40, 44, 48, 52, 56, 60, 64} =: W.
So, if a candidate for K has effective length n, then the existence of the extended
code K′ implies that the system of lemma 226 has a feasible solution for at least one
of the choices 1 ≤ δ ≤ 65 − n and Λ = γ ∈ W . Using Cplex we computationally
check that there are no feasible solutions except for the five codes listed in cases
(a)-(e).
□
We can check by computer that the Barth code KB has a unique shortening to
length 64 which is isomorphic to case (d). So, by theorem 36 this code is realized

200

4. NODAL SEXTICS

by a nodal sextic surface. For the other six cases it remains an open problem
whether they are realized by a nodal sextic surface.
We remark that the approach of the proofs of theorem 227 and theorem 228
is, in principle, also feasible for nodal sextics in P3 with ν ≤ 63 nodes. The
candidates for K can be easily enumerated using LinCode. However, for ν = 63
there are more than 70 000 candidates, so that further coding theoretic criteria
obtained from algebraic geometry methods are desirable.
